The Inherent Difficulty of Definitions
Author asserts the difficulty and rarity of correct definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why definitions are considered resistant to human intellect and why correct definitions are rare.

Core Idea
Correct definitions require extreme effort and are rarely achieved.

In this passage, the author of the *Mi'yār al-'Ilm* emphasizes that definitions are not easy to construct. Even the sharpest minds can fall into confusion. Recognizing this difficulty is the first step to formulating precise definitions in Islamic sciences like fiqh and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
Find the term 'isti'ṣā'' (resistance) in the Arabic. The author claims that definitions resist human power (القوة البشرية) and require utmost exertion (غاية التشمير والجهد).
2
Understand the rarity of success
The phrase 'إلا على الندور' means 'except rarely'. The author asserts that human intellect cannot guard against all sources of confusion in definitions except in rare cases.
The word 'النُّدور' indicates scarcity.
3
Recognize the transition
The passage ends by stating that the most difficult aspects for the mind are four matters, which sets up the next section. Even without knowing them, the main point is clear: definitions are inherently challenging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع : في صعوبة الحد (۲) في استعصاء(۱) الحدّ على القوة البشرية إلا عند غاية التشمير (۲) والجهد. فمن عرف ما ذكرناه في مثارات الاشتباه في الحدّ عرف أن القوة البشرية لا تقوى على التحيّفُظْ عن كلّ ذلك إِلَّا على النُّدور وهي كثيرة وأعصاها على الذهن أربعة أمور :
Chapter Seven: On the difficulty of definition. (2) On the resistance of definition to human power except with utmost exertion and effort. So whoever knows what we have mentioned about sources of confusion in definition knows that human power cannot guard against all of that except rarely, and they are many. And the most difficult for the mind are four matters:

Commentary
This passage introduces the seventh chapter on the difficulty of definitions. The author states that definitions are inherently resistant (استعصاء) to human intellectual power. Only with extreme exertion (التشمير) and effort can one achieve a correct definition. After reviewing the many sources of error just discussed, it becomes clear that human intellect can hardly avoid all mistakes, and success is rare. The passage ends by announcing that the most difficult of these for the mind are four matters, but the text cuts off before listing them. This serves as a transition to the next section.

The Four Obstacles to Perfect Definition
Four obstacles to perfect definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four obstacles to constructing a perfect definition and explain why each makes human-defined definitions fall short.

Core Idea
A perfect definition requires the nearest genus, all essential differentiae, and proper order—yet each is almost impossible for humans to guarantee.

In this passage, the author explains why achieving a perfect definition is extraordinarily difficult. He lists four main obstacles that even the most careful definer may fall into. Understanding these obstacles will sharpen your ability to critique definitions in any Islamic science, from fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four obstacles
List the four obstacles the author mentions: missing the nearest genus, confusing essential differentia with concomitant, incompleteness of differentiae, and improper order of division.
2
Analyze the examples for each
For each obstacle, examine the author's illustrative examples (e.g., wine as 'intoxicating liquid' for the first obstacle). Notice how each example fails the conditions of a perfect definition.
3
Understand why each is difficult to avoid
Reflect on why human intellect tends to make these errors: the nearest genus is not obvious, concomitants mimic differentiae, completeness is hard to achieve, and natural hierarchy is subtle.
4
Evaluate the mutakallimun's alternative
The author notes that theologians settled for a looser definition (al-qaul al-jami' al-mani'). Assess whether this alternative solves the problem or creates new ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في استعصاء الحدّ على القوة البشرية إلا عند غاية التشمير والجهد. فمن عرف ما ذكرناه في مثارات الاشتباه في الحدّ عرف أن القوة البشرية لا تقوى على التحفّظ عن كلّ ذلك إِلَّا على النُّدور وهي كثيرة وأعصاها على الذهن أربعة أمور : أحدها: أنا شرطنا أن نأخذ الجنس الأقرب ومن أين للطالب أن لا يغفل عنه فيأخذ جنساً يظن أنه أقرب، وربما يوجد ما هو أقرب منه فيحدّ الخمر بأنه مائع مسكر ويذهب عن الشراب الذي هو تحته، وهو أقرب منه، ويحدّ الإنسان بأنه ناطق مايت ويغفل عن الحيوان وأمثاله. الثاني : : أنا إذا شرطنا أن تكون الفصول كلّها ذاتية واللازم الذي لا يفارق في الوجود والوهم مشتبه بالذاتي غاية الاشتباه، ودرك ذلك من أغمض الأمور، فمن أين له أن لا يغفل فيأخذ لازماً بدل الفصل فيظن أنه ذاتي؟ الثالث : أنه إذا شرطنا أن نأتي بجميع الفصول الذاتية حتى لا نخل بواحد، ومن أين نأمن من شذوذ واحد عنه لا سيما إذا وجد فصلاً حصل به التمييز والمساواة للاسم في الحمل، كالجسم ذي النفس الحسّاس في مساواته لفظ الحيوان مع أخذ إغفال التحرّك بالإرادة وهذا من أغمض ما يدرك . الرابع : أن الفصل مقوم للنوع ومقسم للجنس، وإذا لم يراع شرط التقسيم في القسمة فصولاً ليست أولية للجنس، وهو عسير غير مرض في الحد. فإن الجسم كما ينقسم إلى النامي وغير النامي انقساماً بفصل ذاتي، فكذلك ينقسم إلى الحسّاس وغير الحسّاس وإلى الناطق وغير الناطق، ولكن مهما قيل : الجسم ينقسم إلى ناطق وغير ناطق، فقد قسم بما ليس الفصل القاسم أولياً، بل ينبغي أن ينقسم أولاً إلى النامي وغير النامي ثم النامي ينقسم إلى الحيوان وغير الحيوان؛ ثم الحيوان إلى الناطق وغير الناطق. وكذلك الحيوان ينقسم إلى ذي رجلين وإلى ذي أرجل، ولكن هذا التقسيم ليس بفصول أولية، بل ينبغي أن يقسم الحيوان إلى ماش وغير ماش، ثم الماشي ينقسم إلى ذي رجلين أو أرجل، إذ الحيوان لم يستعد للرجلين والأرجل باعتبار كونه حيواناً بل باعتبار كونه ماشياً ، واستعد لكونه ماشياً باعتبار كونه حيواناً، فرعاية الترتيب في هذه الأمور شرط للوفاء بصناعة الحدود، وهو في غاية العسر، ولذلك لما عسر ذلك اكتفى المتكلمون بالمميّز فقالوا: «الحدّ هو القول الجامع المانع ولم يشترطوا فيه إلَّا التمييز فيلزم عليه الاكتفاء بذكر الخواص فيقال في حدّ الفرس : أنه الصهال وفي الإنسان : أنه الضحاك، وفي :الكلب أنه النبّاح . وذلك في غاية البعد عن غرض التعرّف لذات المحدود. ولأجل عسر التحديد رأينا أن نورد جملة من الحدود المعلومة المحرّرة في الفن الثاني من كتاب الحد، وقد وقع الفراغ عن الفنّ الأول بحمد الله سبحانه وتعالى.
On the resistance of definition to human power except with extreme effort and exertion. Whoever knows what we have mentioned about the sources of error in definition knows that human power is not strong enough to guard against all of that except rarely, and the most stubborn of them to the mind are four matters: First: we stipulated that we take the nearest genus, and how can the seeker not neglect that and take a genus he thinks is nearest, while perhaps a nearer one exists. So he defines wine as 'intoxicating liquid' and misses the beverage that is below it, which is nearer, and defines human as 'a speaking mortal' and neglects animal and similar. Second: We stipulated that all the differentiae be essential, and the necessary concomitant that never separates in existence and imagination is extremely similar to the essential, and perceiving that is among the most obscure matters. So how can he not neglect and take a concomitant instead of the differentia, thinking it is essential? Third: We stipulated that we bring all the essential differentiae so as not to miss one, and how can we be safe from one missing? Especially if one finds a differentia that gives distinction and equivalence to the name in predication, like 'body with a sentient soul' equaling the word 'animal' while neglecting 'movement by will'. This is among the most obscure things to perceive. Fourth: The differentia is constitutive of the species and divisive of the genus, and if the condition of division is not observed, one divides by differentiae that are not primary for the genus, which is difficult and unsatisfactory in definition. For just as body is divided into growing and non-growing by an essential differentia, so it is divided into sentient and non-sentient and into rational and non-rational. But whenever it is said: 'Body is divided into rational and non-rational,' one has divided by a differentia that is not primary. Rather, it should be divided first into growing and non-growing, then the growing into animal and non-animal, then animal into rational and non-rational. Likewise, animal is divided into two-legged and multi-legged, but this division is not by primary differentiae; rather, animal should be divided into walking and non-walking, then the walking into two-legged or multi-legged, because animal is not prepared for legs insofar as it is animal, but insofar as it is walking, and it is prepared for walking insofar as it is animal. So observing order in these matters is a condition for fulfilling the craft of definitions, and it is extremely difficult. Therefore, since it is difficult, the theologians (mutakallimun) sufficed with the distinguisher and said: 'Definition is the complete and exclusive statement' and they stipulated only distinction, so they relied on mentioning properties. Hence it is said in the definition of horse: 'it is the whinnier,' of human: 'the laugher,' of dog: 'the barker.' That is extremely far from the purpose of knowing the essence of the defined. And because of the difficulty of definition, we intend to present a number of known and verified definitions in the second art of the book on definition, and the first art has been completed by the praise of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage, titled 'The Seventh Chapter: On the Difficulty of Definition,' explains why a perfect definition (hadd) is extremely hard for human intellect. The author first states that definition is 'resistant' (isti'sa') to human power except with extreme effort. After summarizing the earlier sources of error, he declares the most stubborn obstacles are four. The first obstacle: finding the nearest genus (al-jins al-aqrab). The definer may pick a remote genus, like defining wine as 'intoxicating liquid' instead of 'beverage' which is nearer, or defining human as 'speaking mortal' instead of 'animal' which is nearer. The second obstacle: distinguishing essential differentiae from necessary concomitants. Necessary concomitants (lazim) closely resemble essential differentiae, so one might mistakenly use a concomitant as the differentia. The third obstacle: completeness. One must bring all essential differentiae; missing even one renders the definition incomplete. The author notes that even if a single differentia gives distinction and co-extension, like 'body with a sentient soul' for animal, one might neglect 'movement by will' which is also essential. The fourth obstacle: maintaining the proper order of division. Differentiae must be primary for the genus; they should follow the natural hierarchy of division. For example, body should first be divided into growing and non-growing, then growing into animal and non-animal, then animal into rational and non-rational. Skipping levels (e.g., directly dividing body into rational and non-rational) violates the order. Similarly, animal should be divided into walking and non-walking before further division by legs. This order is very difficult to observe. Due to these difficulties, the theologians (mutakallimun) settled for a looser definition: 'a complete and exclusive statement' (al-qaul al-jami' al-mani'), which only requires distinction, not essence. This leads to defining by properties (khawass), like 'horse is the whinnier,' etc., which is far from the goal of knowing the essence. The author announces he will provide some accurate definitions in the next art.

Key Terms
الجنس الأقرب
فصل ذاتي
لازم
تقسيم
فصول أولية
قول جامع مانع

The Nearest Genus
First difficulty: taking the nearest genus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the nearest genus of a definiendum and avoid selecting a remote genus in definitions.

Core Idea
The nearest genus is the immediate container of the species.

The first obstacle in constructing a perfect definition is selecting the correct genus. The author warns against taking a genus that is not the nearest, as when wine is defined as 'intoxicating liquid' instead of 'intoxicating beverage.' This lesson will train you to spot such errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the passage. For example, 'wine' or 'man'.
2
List possible genera
Think of all categories that include the definiendum. For wine: liquid, beverage, intoxicating substance.
3
Order genera by proximity
Arrange them from most general to most specific. The nearest genus is the one immediately above the species.
For wine: liquid → beverage → intoxicating beverage. 'Beverage' is nearer than 'liquid'.
4
Check the author's example
The author criticizes defining wine as 'intoxicating liquid' because 'beverage' is a nearer genus that is overlooked.
Similarly for man: 'speaking mortal' misses the genus 'animal'.
5
Apply to a new case
Practice with an Islamic legal term like 'salah' (prayer). Its nearest genus could be 'worship act' or 'bodily worship'? Determine the immediate genus.
This skill is essential in fiqh to avoid overbroad definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أنا شرطنا أن نأخذ الجنس الأقرب ومن أين للطالب أن لا يغفل عنه فيأخذ جنساً يظن أنه أقرب، وربما يوجد ما هو أقرب منه فيحدّ الخمر بأنه مائع مسكر ويذهب عن الشراب الذي هو تحته، وهو أقرب منه، ويحدّ الإنسان بأنه ناطق مايت ويغفل عن الحيوان وأمثاله.
الثاني: أنا إذا شرطنا أن تكون الفصول كلّها ذاتية واللازم الذي لا يفارق في الوجود والوهم مشتبه بالذاتي غاية الاشتباه، ودرك ذلك من أغمض الأمور، فمن أين له أن لا يغفل فيأخذ لازماً بدل الفصل فيظن أنه ذاتي؟
الثالث: أنه إذا شرطنا أن نأتي بجميع الفصول الذاتية حتى لا نخل بواحد، ومن أين نأمن من شذوذ واحد عنه لا سيما إذا وجد فصلاً حصل به التمييز والمساواة للاسم في الحمل، كالجسم ذي النفس الحسّاس في مساواته لفظ الحيوان مع إغفال التحرّك بالإرادة وهذا من أغمض ما يدرك.
الرابع: أن الفصل مقوم للنوع ومقسم للجنس، وإذا لم يراع شرط التقسيم في القسمة فصولاً ليست أولية للجنس، وهو عسير غير مرض في الحد. فإن الجسم كما ينقسم إلى النامي وغير النامي انقساماً بفصل ذاتي، فكذلك ينقسم إلى الحسّاس وغير الحسّاس وإلى الناطق وغير الناطق، ولكن مهما قيل: الجسم ينقسم إلى ناطق وغير ناطق، فقد قسم بما ليس الفصل القاسم أولياً، بل ينبغي أن ينقسم أولاً إلى النامي وغير النامي ثم النامي ينقسم إلى الحيوان وغير الحيوان؛ ثم الحيوان إلى الناطق وغير الناطق. وكذلك الحيوان ينقسم إلى ذي رجلين وإلى ذي أرجل، ولكن هذا التقسيم ليس بفصول أولية، بل ينبغي أن يقسم الحيوان إلى ماش وغير ماش، ثم الماشي ينقسم إلى ذي رجلين أو أرجل، إذ الحيوان لم يستعد للرجلين والأرجل باعتبار كونه حيواناً بل باعتبار كونه ماشياً، واستعد لكونه ماشياً باعتبار كونه حيواناً، فرعاية الترتيب في هذه الأمور شرط للوفاء بصناعة الحدود، وهو في غاية العسر، ولذلك لما عسر ذلك اكتفى المتكلمون بالمميّز فقالوا: «الحدّ هو القول الجامع المانع ولم يشترطوا فيه إلَّا التمييز فيلزم عليه الاكتفاء بذكر الخواص فيقال في حدّ الفرس: أنه الصهال وفي الإنسان: أنه الضحاك، وفي الكلب أنه النبّاح. وذلك في غاية البعد عن غرض التعرّف لذات المحدود. ولأجل عسر التحديد رأينا أن نورد جملة من الحدود المعلومة المحرّرة في الفن الثاني من كتاب الحد، وقد وقع الفراغ عن الفنّ الأول بحمد الله سبحانه وتعالى.
First: We have stipulated that we take the nearest genus. How can the student not overlook it and take a genus he thinks is nearest, while perhaps there is something nearer? So he defines wine as 'an intoxicating liquid' and misses the 'beverage' which is under it and is nearer. He defines man as 'a speaking mortal' and overlooks 'animal' and its likes. Second: When we stipulate that all differentiate be essential, and the inseparable concomitant in existence and imagination is extremely similar to the essential, and grasping that is among the most obscure matters, how can he not overlook and take a concomitant instead of the differentia, thinking it is essential? Third: When we stipulate that we bring all essential differentiate so as not to omit one, how can we be safe from one escaping, especially if he finds a differentia that gives distinction and equal coverage in predication, like 'body with a sensitive soul' in its equal coverage of the word 'animal' while omitting 'voluntary movement'? This is among the most obscure things to grasp. Fourth: The differentia constitutes the species and divides the genus. If one does not observe the condition of division in the division, [one may obtain] differentiate that are not primary for the genus, which is difficult and unsatisfactory in definition. For body, just as it is divided into growing and non-growing by an essential differentia, so also it is divided into sensitive and non-sensitive, and into rational and non-rational. But whenever it is said: 'body is divided into rational and non-rational,' one has divided by what is not a primary dividing differentia. Rather, it should first be divided into growing and non-growing, then the growing into animal and non-animal, then animal into rational and non-rational. Likewise, animal is divided into biped and quadruped, but this division is not by primary differentiate. Rather, animal should be divided into walking and non-walking, then the walking into biped or quadruped, since animal is not prepared for legs qua animal but qua walking, and it is prepared for walking qua animal. Observing order in these matters is a condition for fulfilling the art of definitions, and it is extremely difficult. Therefore, when it became difficult, the theologians were content with the distinguisher and said: 'Definition is the comprehensive and exclusive statement,' and they only required distinction. Consequently, one is content with mentioning properties, saying in defining a horse: 'it is the neighing one,' in man: 'the laughing one,' in dog: 'the barking one.' That is extremely far from the purpose of attaining knowledge of the essence of the defined. And because of the difficulty of definition, we decided to present a number of well-known, well-constructed definitions in the second art of the book of definition. And the first art has been completed, praise be to Allah, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage enumerates four difficulties in constructing a proper definition (hadd) according to the strict conditions of the logicians. The author explains why perfect definition is exceedingly hard and why many theologians settled for a simpler 'comprehensive and exclusive' statement. The first difficulty concerns selecting the nearest genus (al-jins al-aqrab). The logician must take the closest genus, but the student might take a more remote genus, e.g., defining wine as 'an intoxicating liquid' instead of 'an intoxicating beverage,' since 'beverage' is a nearer genus. Similarly, defining man as 'speaking mortal' misses the genus 'animal.' The second difficulty is distinguishing essential differentiate from inseparable concomitants. For example, 'risibility' is a concomitant of man, not a differentia. The student might mistake a concomitant for an essential property. The third difficulty is ensuring all essential differentiate are included. One might omit an essential differentia because it is not obvious, like missing 'voluntary movement' in the definition of animal. The fourth difficulty is the proper order of division. Differentia must be primary to the genus. For instance, body should first be divided by 'growing' before 'sensitive,' because 'sensitive' presupposes 'growing' (since animals are growing bodies). Dividing body directly into 'rational' and 'non-rational' skips necessary intermediate differentiate. The author concludes that due to these difficulties, the theologians (mutakallimun) adopted a minimal definition: 'the comprehensive and exclusive statement' (al-qawl al-jami' al-mani'), which only requires distinguishing the definiendum from others. This leads to definitions like 'man is the laughing animal,' which do not reveal essence. Therefore, the author will present corrected definitions in the second art.

Key Terms
الجنس الأقرب
الحد

Key Takeaway
Selecting a remote genus leads to an incomplete definition; always seek the immediate container of the species.

Essential Differentiae vs Inseparable Concomitants
Second difficulty: distinguishing essential differentiate from inseparable concomitants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish an essential differentia from an inseparable concomitant in a definition.

Core Idea
Not every inseparable property is essential; many are mere concomitants.

The second difficulty in definition is the confusion between essential differentiae and inseparable concomitants (lazim). The author notes that properties like 'laughing' for man are not essential, yet are often mistaken for differentiae. This lesson will sharpen your ability to tell them apart.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definiendum
We are defining a species, e.g., 'man'.
2
List its inseparable properties
Properties that always accompany the species, such as 'laughing', 'writing', 'having a thumb' for man.
These are lazim, not necessarily essential.
3
Identify the essential differentia
The differentia is the property that constitutes the species and divides the genus. For man, 'rational' is essential.
Essential differentiate are discovered through analysis of the species' essence.
4
Check the author's example
The author warns that the student might take a concomitant like 'laughing' (which is a lazim) instead of the true differentia 'rational'.
Laughing is an inseparable property but not essential.
5
Apply to another species
For the horse, its essential differentia is not 'neighing' but something like 'hoofed herbivore' or a specific genus-differentia combination. Neighing is a lazim.
This is critical in defining living kinds correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها: أنا شرطنا أن نأخذ الجنس الأقرب ومن أين للطالب أن لا يغفل عنه فيأخذ جنساً يظن أنه أقرب، وربما يوجد ما هو أقرب منه فيحدّ الخمر بأنه مائع مسكر ويذهب عن الشراب الذي هو تحته، وهو أقرب منه، ويحدّ الإنسان بأنه ناطق مايت ويغفل عن الحيوان وأمثاله.
الثاني: أنا إذا شرطنا أن تكون الفصول كلّها ذاتية واللازم الذي لا يفارق في الوجود والوهم مشتبه بالذاتي غاية الاشتباه، ودرك ذلك من أغمض الأمور، فمن أين له أن لا يغفل فيأخذ لازماً بدل الفصل فيظن أنه ذاتي؟
الثالث: أنه إذا شرطنا أن نأتي بجميع الفصول الذاتية حتى لا نخل بواحد، ومن أين نأمن من شذوذ واحد عنه لا سيما إذا وجد فصلاً حصل به التمييز والمساواة للاسم في الحمل، كالجسم ذي النفس الحسّاس في مساواته لفظ الحيوان مع إغفال التحرّك بالإرادة وهذا من أغمض ما يدرك.
الرابع: أن الفصل مقوم للنوع ومقسم للجنس، وإذا لم يراع شرط التقسيم في القسمة فصولاً ليست أولية للجنس، وهو عسير غير مرض في الحد. فإن الجسم كما ينقسم إلى النامي وغير النامي انقساماً بفصل ذاتي، فكذلك ينقسم إلى الحسّاس وغير الحسّاس وإلى الناطق وغير الناطق، ولكن مهما قيل: الجسم ينقسم إلى ناطق وغير ناطق، فقد قسم بما ليس الفصل القاسم أولياً، بل ينبغي أن ينقسم أولاً إلى النامي وغير النامي ثم النامي ينقسم إلى الحيوان وغير الحيوان؛ ثم الحيوان إلى الناطق وغير الناطق. وكذلك الحيوان ينقسم إلى ذي رجلين وإلى ذي أرجل، ولكن هذا التقسيم ليس بفصول أولية، بل ينبغي أن يقسم الحيوان إلى ماش وغير ماش، ثم الماشي ينقسم إلى ذي رجلين أو أرجل، إذ الحيوان لم يستعد للرجلين والأرجل باعتبار كونه حيواناً بل باعتبار كونه ماشياً، واستعد لكونه ماشياً باعتبار كونه حيواناً، فرعاية الترتيب في هذه الأمور شرط للوفاء بصناعة الحدود، وهو في غاية العسر، ولذلك لما عسر ذلك اكتفى المتكلمون بالمميّز فقالوا: «الحدّ هو القول الجامع المانع ولم يشترطوا فيه إلَّا التمييز فيلزم عليه الاكتفاء بذكر الخواص فيقال في حدّ الفرس: أنه الصهال وفي الإنسان: أنه الضحاك، وفي الكلب أنه النبّاح. وذلك في غاية البعد عن غرض التعرّف لذات المحدود. ولأجل عسر التحديد رأينا أن نورد جملة من الحدود المعلومة المحرّرة في الفن الثاني من كتاب الحد، وقد وقع الفراغ عن الفنّ الأول بحمد الله سبحانه وتعالى.
First: We have stipulated that we take the nearest genus. How can the student not overlook it and take a genus he thinks is nearest, while perhaps there is something nearer? So he defines wine as 'an intoxicating liquid' and misses the 'beverage' which is under it and is nearer. He defines man as 'a speaking mortal' and overlooks 'animal' and its likes. Second: When we stipulate that all differentiate be essential, and the inseparable concomitant in existence and imagination is extremely similar to the essential, and grasping that is among the most obscure matters, how can he not overlook and take a concomitant instead of the differentia, thinking it is essential? Third: When we stipulate that we bring all essential differentiate so as not to omit one, how can we be safe from one escaping, especially if he finds a differentia that gives distinction and equal coverage in predication, like 'body with a sensitive soul' in its equal coverage of the word 'animal' while omitting 'voluntary movement'? This is among the most obscure things to grasp. Fourth: The differentia constitutes the species and divides the genus. If one does not observe the condition of division in the division, [one may obtain] differentiate that are not primary for the genus, which is difficult and unsatisfactory in definition. For body, just as it is divided into growing and non-growing by an essential differentia, so also it is divided into sensitive and non-sensitive, and into rational and non-rational. But whenever it is said: 'body is divided into rational and non-rational,' one has divided by what is not a primary dividing differentia. Rather, it should first be divided into growing and non-growing, then the growing into animal and non-animal, then animal into rational and non-rational. Likewise, animal is divided into biped and quadruped, but this division is not by primary differentiate. Rather, animal should be divided into walking and non-walking, then the walking into biped or quadruped, since animal is not prepared for legs qua animal but qua walking, and it is prepared for walking qua animal. Observing order in these matters is a condition for fulfilling the art of definitions, and it is extremely difficult. Therefore, when it became difficult, the theologians were content with the distinguisher and said: 'Definition is the comprehensive and exclusive statement,' and they only required distinction. Consequently, one is content with mentioning properties, saying in defining a horse: 'it is the neighing one,' in man: 'the laughing one,' in dog: 'the barking one.' That is extremely far from the purpose of attaining knowledge of the essence of the defined. And because of the difficulty of definition, we decided to present a number of well-known, well-constructed definitions in the second art of the book of definition. And the first art has been completed, praise be to Allah, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage enumerates four difficulties in constructing a proper definition (hadd) according to the strict conditions of the logicians. The author explains why perfect definition is exceedingly hard and why many theologians settled for a simpler 'comprehensive and exclusive' statement. The first difficulty concerns selecting the nearest genus (al-jins al-aqrab). The logician must take the closest genus, but the student might take a more remote genus, e.g., defining wine as 'an intoxicating liquid' instead of 'an intoxicating beverage,' since 'beverage' is a nearer genus. Similarly, defining man as 'speaking mortal' misses the genus 'animal.' The second difficulty is distinguishing essential differentiate from inseparable concomitants. For example, 'risibility' is a concomitant of man, not a differentia. The student might mistake a concomitant for an essential property. The third difficulty is ensuring all essential differentiate are included. One might omit an essential differentia because it is not obvious, like missing 'voluntary movement' in the definition of animal. The fourth difficulty is the proper order of division. Differentia must be primary to the genus. For instance, body should first be divided by 'growing' before 'sensitive,' because 'sensitive' presupposes 'growing' (since animals are growing bodies). Dividing body directly into 'rational' and 'non-rational' skips necessary intermediate differentiate. The author concludes that due to these difficulties, the theologians (mutakallimun) adopted a minimal definition: 'the comprehensive and exclusive statement' (al-qawl al-jami' al-mani'), which only requires distinguishing the definiendum from others. This leads to definitions like 'man is the laughing animal,' which do not reveal essence. Therefore, the author will present corrected definitions in the second art.

لازم

Key Takeaway
Essential differentiate constitute the species; inseparable concomitants, though always present, do not reveal the essence.

The Proper Order of Division in Definitions
Fourth difficulty: proper order of division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to order differentiate correctly by starting with the most primary divisions of the genus.

Core Idea
Differentiae must be primary to the genus; skip no intermediate divisions.

The fourth difficulty concerns the order of division: the differentia must be primary to the genus. The author illustrates with the example of body: it should be divided first by 'growing' before 'sensitive' or 'rational'. This lesson teaches the proper sequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus to be divided
Start with the broadest category, e.g., 'body' (jism).
2
Find the essential divisions of that genus
Body divides first into 'growing' and 'non-growing' — this is the most primary differentia.
Differentia must be immediate: 'growing' is a property that directly divides body.
3
Then divide each sub-genus further
From 'growing body', divide into 'sensitive' (animal) and 'non-sensitive' (plant). Then from 'sensitive', divide into 'rational' and 'non-rational'.
Skipping steps, e.g., dividing body directly into 'rational' and 'non-rational', yields non-primary differentiate.
4
Check the author's example
The author says dividing body into 'rational and non-rational' is wrong because 'rational' is not a primary division of body; it is a division of 'animal'.
Similarly, dividing animal into 'biped' and 'quadruped' skips the intermediate division 'walking'.
5
Apply to a new genus
Consider the genus 'substance' (jawhar). What is its primary division? It divides into 'material' and 'immaterial'? Then material into 'growing'? This shows the hierarchical order.
This is crucial in constructing a definition that mirrors the real order of essence.

Two Purposes of Detailed Definitions
Author explains why he provides many detailed definitions despite impossibility of completeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two benefits of presenting detailed definitions in logic and explain how each serves the student.

Core Idea
Detailed definitions build skill and equip you for debate.

The author acknowledges that an exhaustive list of definitions is impossible, yet he includes a collection of detailed definitions. Why? He gives two clear reasons that are both practical and strategic. Understanding these reasons helps you appreciate why logic texts often include illustrative definitions even when they cannot cover every term.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the problem of infinite definables
The author states that things that can be defined are limitless because the assenting sciences (al-‘ulūm al-taṣdīqiyya) are infinite and depend on conceptual sciences. This sets up the need for a selective approach.
Assent (taṣdīq) always involves at least two concepts (taṣawwurāt), so definitions underlie all knowledge.
2
Extract the first benefit
The first benefit (al-fā'ida al-ūlā) is to gain practice (al-durba) in the method of formulating and composing definitions. Practice strengthens ability, as with any skill.
3
Extract the second benefit
The second benefit is to acquaint yourself with the meanings of terms used by philosophers, especially those discussed in the author's Tahāfut al-Falāsifa. Understanding their language is necessary for effective debate.
4
Connect to your own study
Reflect on how these two benefits apply to your learning: you are practising logical definition, and you are learning the technical vocabulary of Islamic philosophy and kalam.
This dual purpose makes the definitions in this section a practical tool, not a theoretical exercise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني: في الحدود المفصلة. اعلم أن الأشياء التي يمكن تحديدها لا نهاية لها، لأن العلوم التصديقية غير متناهية وهي تابعة للتصوّرية، فأقل ما يشتمل عليه التصديقي تصوّران، وعلى الجملة فكلّ ما له اسم يمكن تحرير حدّه أو رسمه أو شرح اسمه، وإذا لم يكن في الاستقصاء مطمع فالأولى الاقتصار على القوانين المُعرفة لطريقه، وقد حصل ذلك بالفنّ الأول. ولكن أوردنا حدوداً مفصلة لفائدتين: إحدهما: أن تحصل الدُّرْبَة بكيفية تحرير الحدّ وتأليفه، فإن الامتحان والممارسة للشيء تفيد قوة عليه لا محالة. والثاني: أن يقع الاطلاع على معاني أسماء أطلقها الفلاسفة، وقد أوردناها في كتاب تهافت الفلاسفة إذ لم يمكن مناظرتهم إلا بلغتهم وعلى حكم اصطلاحهم، وإذا لم يفهم ما أرادوه لا يمكن مناظرتهم. فقد أوردنا حدود ألفاظ أطلقوها فى الإلهيات والطبيعيات وشيئاً قليلاً من الرياضيات، فَلتُؤخذ هذه الحدود على أنها شرح للاسم فإن قام البرهان على أن ما شرحوه هو كما شرحوه اعتقد حداً، وإلَّا اعتقد شرحاً للاسم، كما نقول: حدّ الجن حيوان هوائي ناطق مُشفّ شأنه أن يتشكّل بأشكال مختلفة، فيكون هذا شرحاً للاسم في تفاهم الناس. فأما هذا وجود الشيء على هذا الوجه فيعرف بالبرهان، فإن دلّ على وجوده كان حدًا بحسب الذات، وإن لم يدلّ عليه بل دلّ على أن الجنّ المراد في الشرع الموصوف بوصفه أمر آخر، أخذ هذا شرحاً للاسم في تفاهم الناس. وكما نقول في حدّ الخلاء: إنه يُعدّ يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة، قائم لا في مادة، جسم ويخلو عنه. وربما يدلّ الدليل على أن ذلك محال [الجرم من شأنه أن يملأه]
The Second Art: On Detailed Definitions. Know that the things that can be defined are limitless, because assenting sciences are infinite and they depend on conceptual sciences; the least that an assent contains are two concepts. In general, everything that has a name can have its definition, description, or explanation of its name formulated. Since there is no hope of exhaustive coverage, it is better to confine ourselves to the rules that make its method known, and that has been achieved in the first art. However, we have presented detailed definitions for two benefits: One: To gain practice in the method of formulating and composing definitions, for testing and practicing a thing inevitably gives strength in it. The second: To become acquainted with the meanings of terms used by the philosophers, which we have presented in the book 'Tahafut al-Falasifa', since it is not possible to debate them except in their language and according to their conventions, and if one does not understand what they intend, one cannot debate them. So we have presented definitions of terms they used in theology (ilāhiyyāt), physics (tabī‘iyyāt), and a little from mathematics (riyāḍiyyāt). Let these definitions be taken as explanations of the name (sharḥ al-ism); for if demonstration (burhān) establishes that what they explained is as they explained it, it is considered a real definition (ḥadd). Otherwise, it is considered an explanation of the name. For example, we define jinn as 'a gaseous, rational, transparent animal whose nature is to assume various forms.' This would be an explanation of the name for common understanding. As for the existence of the thing in this manner, it is known by demonstration. If demonstration indicates its existence, then it is a definition by essence; if it does not indicate it, but rather indicates that the jinn intended in the revealed law described by another description is something else, then this is taken as an explanation of the name in common understanding. And similarly we say in defining void (khalā’): it is something in which three dimensions can be supposed, existing without a material substrate, and [body] would fill it. Perhaps proof indicates that this is impossible, [for the body is such that it would fill it].
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage introduces the Second Art of the book, 'On Detailed Definitions.' The author explains his rationale for providing a collection of definitions despite the impossibility of exhaustive coverage. He notes that things definable are infinite because even assenting sciences (al-‘ulūm al-taṣdīqiyya) are infinite and depend on conceptual sciences (al-taṣawwuriyya). Since one cannot list all definitions, the first art already gave the rules. However, he includes detailed definitions for two benefits. The first benefit is to give the student practice (al-durba) in the skill of constructing definitions, as practice strengthens ability. The second benefit is to clarify the meanings of technical terms used by the philosophers (al-falāsifa), particularly those he has discussed in his book 'Tahāfut al-Falāsifa' (The Incoherence of the Philosophers). Because one cannot engage in debate with philosophers without understanding their terminology, he provides definitions of their terms in theology, physics, and a bit of mathematics. He then makes a crucial distinction: these definitions are to be taken as explanations of the name (sharḥ al-ism), not necessarily as real definitions (ḥadd). If a demonstration (burhān) proves that the thing exists as described, then it is a real definition. Otherwise, it remains an explanation of the name. He gives two examples: (1) the definition of jinn as 'a gaseous, rational, transparent animal capable of assuming various forms' — this is an explanation of the name for common understanding; whether such entities exist in reality is known by demonstration, and if the revealed concept of jinn differs, then the definition is merely a linguistic explanation. (2) The definition of void (khalā') as 'that in which three dimensions can be supposed, existing without a material substrate, and which a body would fill' — here too, if proof shows void is impossible, then this definition is only an explanation of the name, not a real definition.

Explanation of the Name vs. Real Definition
Author distinguishes between sharḥ al-ism and ḥadd using examples of jinn and void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sharḥ al-ism and ḥadd by checking whether a definition is backed by demonstration (burhān).

Core Idea
A definition is real only if demonstration confirms the thing exists as described.

The author makes a crucial distinction: the definitions he provides are not necessarily real definitions (ḥudūd ḥaqīqiyya) but rather explanations of the name (sharḥ al-ism). The difference depends on whether a demonstration (burhān) proves that the thing exists in reality exactly as defined. This lesson unpacks that distinction using the examples of jinn and void.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default status: sharḥ al-ism
The author says 'let these definitions be taken as explanations of the name (sharḥ al-ism).' This means they clarify the meaning of a term for common understanding, without claiming that the term refers to something real.
Sharḥ al-ism is a linguistic or conceptual clarification, not a ontological claim.
2
Identify the condition for elevation to ḥadd
If a demonstration (burhān) proves that what they explained exists exactly as described, then it becomes a real definition (ḥadd). Otherwise, it remains a mere explanation.
Burhān is a demonstrative proof that establishes existence and essence.
3
Analyze the jinn example
The definition 'jinn is a gaseous, rational, transparent animal that can assume forms' is an explanation of the name for common understanding. Whether such beings exist in reality depends on demonstration, and if the revealed description of jinn differs, then this definition is not a real definition.
The author shows that even a detailed definition can be only sharḥ al-ism if not confirmed.
4
Analyze the void (khalā') example
Similarly, defining void as 'something in which three dimensions can be supposed, existing without a material substrate' is an explanation of the name. If proof shows void is impossible, then the definition is not real.
This example illustrates that definitions of disputed entities are often provisional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني: في الحدود المفصلة. اعلم أن الأشياء التي يمكن تحديدها لا نهاية لها، لأن العلوم التصديقية غير متناهية وهي تابعة للتصوّرية، فأقل ما يشتمل عليه التصديقي تصوّران، وعلى الجملة فكلّ ما له اسم يمكن تحرير حدّه أو رسمه أو شرح اسمه، وإذا لم يكن في الاستقصاء مطمع فالأولى الاقتصار على القوانين المُعرفة لطريقه، وقد حصل ذلك بالفنّ الأول. ولكن أوردنا حدوداً مفصلة لفائدتين: إحدهما: أن تحصل الدُّرْبَة بكيفية تحرير الحدّ وتأليفه، فإن الامتحان والممارسة للشيء تفيد قوة عليه لا محالة. والثاني: أن يقع الاطلاع على معاني أسماء أطلقها الفلاسفة، وقد أوردناها في كتاب تهافت الفلاسفة إذ لم يمكن مناظرتهم إلا بلغتهم وعلى حكم اصطلاحهم، وإذا لم يفهم ما أرادوه لا يمكن مناظرتهم. فقد أوردنا حدود ألفاظ أطلقوها فى الإلهيات والطبيعيات وشيئاً قليلاً من الرياضيات، فَلتُؤخذ هذه الحدود على أنها شرح للاسم فإن قام البرهان على أن ما شرحوه هو كما شرحوه اعتقد حداً، وإلَّا اعتقد شرحاً للاسم، كما نقول: حدّ الجن حيوان هوائي ناطق مُشفّ شأنه أن يتشكّل بأشكال مختلفة، فيكون هذا شرحاً للاسم في تفاهم الناس. فأما هذا وجود الشيء على هذا الوجه فيعرف بالبرهان، فإن دلّ على وجوده كان حدًا بحسب الذات، وإن لم يدلّ عليه بل دلّ على أن الجنّ المراد في الشرع الموصوف بوصفه أمر آخر، أخذ هذا شرحاً للاسم في تفاهم الناس. وكما نقول في حدّ الخلاء: إنه يُعدّ يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة، قائم لا في مادة، جسم ويخلو عنه. وربما يدلّ الدليل على أن ذلك محال [الجرم من شأنه أن يملأه]
The Second Art: On Detailed Definitions. Know that the things that can be defined are limitless, because assenting sciences are infinite and they depend on conceptual sciences; the least that an assent contains are two concepts. In general, everything that has a name can have its definition, description, or explanation of its name formulated. Since there is no hope of exhaustive coverage, it is better to confine ourselves to the rules that make its method known, and that has been achieved in the first art. However, we have presented detailed definitions for two benefits: One: To gain practice in the method of formulating and composing definitions, for testing and practicing a thing inevitably gives strength in it. The second: To become acquainted with the meanings of terms used by the philosophers, which we have presented in the book 'Tahafut al-Falasifa', since it is not possible to debate them except in their language and according to their conventions, and if one does not understand what they intend, one cannot debate them. So we have presented definitions of terms they used in theology (ilāhiyyāt), physics (tabī‘iyyāt), and a little from mathematics (riyāḍiyyāt). Let these definitions be taken as explanations of the name (sharḥ al-ism); for if demonstration (burhān) establishes that what they explained is as they explained it, it is considered a real definition (ḥadd). Otherwise, it is considered an explanation of the name. For example, we define jinn as 'a gaseous, rational, transparent animal whose nature is to assume various forms.' This would be an explanation of the name for common understanding. As for the existence of the thing in this manner, it is known by demonstration. If demonstration indicates its existence, then it is a definition by essence; if it does not indicate it, but rather indicates that the jinn intended in the revealed law described by another description is something else, then this is taken as an explanation of the name in common understanding. And similarly we say in defining void (khalā’): it is something in which three dimensions can be supposed, existing without a material substrate, and [body] would fill it. Perhaps proof indicates that this is impossible, [for the body is such that it would fill it].
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage introduces the Second Art of the book, 'On Detailed Definitions.' The author explains his rationale for providing a collection of definitions despite the impossibility of exhaustive coverage. He notes that things definable are infinite because even assenting sciences (al-‘ulūm al-taṣdīqiyya) are infinite and depend on conceptual sciences (al-taṣawwuriyya). Since one cannot list all definitions, the first art already gave the rules. However, he includes detailed definitions for two benefits. The first benefit is to give the student practice (al-durba) in the skill of constructing definitions, as practice strengthens ability. The second benefit is to clarify the meanings of technical terms used by the philosophers (al-falāsifa), particularly those he has discussed in his book 'Tahāfut al-Falāsifa' (The Incoherence of the Philosophers). Because one cannot engage in debate with philosophers without understanding their terminology, he provides definitions of their terms in theology, physics, and a bit of mathematics. He then makes a crucial distinction: these definitions are to be taken as explanations of the name (sharḥ al-ism), not necessarily as real definitions (ḥadd). If a demonstration (burhān) proves that the thing exists as described, then it is a real definition. Otherwise, it remains an explanation of the name. He gives two examples: (1) the definition of jinn as 'a gaseous, rational, transparent animal capable of assuming various forms' — this is an explanation of the name for common understanding; whether such entities exist in reality is known by demonstration, and if the revealed concept of jinn differs, then the definition is merely a linguistic explanation. (2) The definition of void (khalā') as 'that in which three dimensions can be supposed, existing without a material substrate, and which a body would fill' — here too, if proof shows void is impossible, then this definition is only an explanation of the name, not a real definition.

Purpose and Status of Detailed Definitions
Author introduces the second art on detailed definitions, explaining two benefits and providing examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real definition (hadd) and an explanation of a name (sharh al-ism) and explain the two benefits of studying detailed definitions.

Core Idea
Detailed definitions are not all real; some are just explanations of names until proven by demonstration.

This passage opens the second art of logic on detailed definitions. The author explains why he includes them—for practice and to understand philosophers' terminology—and crucially warns that these definitions are not automatically real; they depend on proof. This distinction is essential for anyone who wants to engage in rigorous theological or philosophical debate without conflating linguistic usage with reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the infinite scope of definitions
The author claims that definable things are infinite because assenting sciences are infinite and depend on concepts. This sets up the need for selective, practice-oriented definitions rather than exhaustive coverage.
This is a pragmatic justification for focusing on method over enumeration.
2
Extract the two benefits of studying detailed definitions
First benefit: gaining practice (durbah) in formulating definitions. Second benefit: understanding the technical terms used by philosophers, which is necessary for debating them.
The second benefit ties directly to the author's own work Tahafut al-Falasifah.
3
Trace the distinction between hadd and sharh al-ism
The author states that definitions presented should be taken as sharh al-ism (explanation of the name) unless proof (burhan) establishes that they match reality, in which case they become hadd (real definition).
This is a key epistemological safeguard against confusing convention with truth.
4
Analyze the example of jinn
The definition 'airy, speaking, transparent animal that can assume forms' is presented as sharh al-ism for common understanding. Whether it is a real definition depends on proof of existence and essence.
The same term might refer to something else in revealed law.
5
Analyze the example of void
Void is defined as a dimension in which three dimensions can be supposed. The author hints that evidence may show this is impossible, so it remains a mere name explanation.
This shows the provisional nature of philosophical definitions.

الفن الثاني في الحدود المفصلة
اعلم أن الأشياء التي يمكن تحديدها لا نهاية لها، لأن العلوم التصديقية غير متناهية وهي تابعة للتصوّرية، فأقل ما يشتمل عليه التصديقي تصوّران، وعلى الجملة فكلّ ما له اسم يمكن تحرير حدّه أو رسمه أو شرح اسمه، وإذا لم يكن في الاستقصاء مطمع فالأولى الاقتصار على القوانين المُعرفة لطريقه، وقد حصل ذلك بالفنّ الأول، ولكن أوردنا حدوداً مفصلة لفائدتين:
إحداهما: أن تحصل الدُّرْبَة بكيفية تحرير الحدّ وتأليفه، فإن الامتحان والممارسة للشيء تفيد قوة عليه لا محالة.
والثاني: أن يقع الاطلاع على معاني أسماء أطلقها الفلاسفة، وقد أوردناها في كتاب تهافت الفلاسفة إذ لم يمكن مناظرتهم إلا بلغتهم وعلى حكم اصطلاحهم، وإذا لم يفهم ما أرادوه لا يمكن مناظرتهم، فقد أوردنا حدود ألفاظ أطلقوها في الإلهيات والطبيعيات وشيئاً قليلاً من الرياضيات، فَلتُؤخذ هذه الحدود على أنها شرح للاسم فإن قام البرهان على أن ما شرحوه هو كما شرحوه اعتقد حداً، وإلّا اعتقد شرحاً للاسم، كما نقول: حدّ الجن حيوان هوائي ناطق مُشفّ شأنه أن يتشكّل بأشكال مختلفة، فيكون هذا شرحاً للاسم في تفاهم الناس. فأما هذا وجود الشيء على هذا الوجه فيعرف بالبرهان، فإن دلّ على وجوده كان حدًا بحسب الذات، وإن لم يدلّ عليه بل دلّ على أن الجنّ المراد في الشرع الموصوف بوصفه أمر آخر، أخذ هذا شرحاً للاسم في تفاهم الناس، وكما نقول في حدّ الخلاء: إنه يُعدّ يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة، جسم قائم لا في مادة، وربما يدلّ الدليل على أن ذلك محال من الجرم، من شأنه أن يملأه ويخلو عنه. وإنما قدمنا هذه المقدمة لتعلم أن ما نورده من الحدود شرحاً لما أراده الفلاسفة بالإطلاق، لا حكم بأن ما ذكروه هو على ما ذكروه، فإن ذلك ربما يتوقف على النظر في موجب البرهان عليه.
The second art: on detailed definitions. Know that things which can be defined are infinite, because assenting sciences are infinite and they are subordinate to conceptual sciences, so the least that an assenting [proposition] contains is two concepts. In summary, everything that has a name can have its definition, description, or explanation of its name formulated. Since there is no hope in exhaustive coverage, it is better to confine oneself to the laws that inform about its method. That was achieved in the first art. But we have presented detailed definitions for two benefits: one: that practice is gained in the manner of formulating a definition and composing it, for testing and exercising something without doubt gives strength to it. The second: that knowledge is gained of the meanings of names that the philosophers have used, and we have presented them in the book Tahafut al-Falasifah, since it is not possible to debate them except in their language and according to their terminology. If what they intended is not understood, it is not possible to debate them. So we have presented definitions of terms they used in theology, physics, and a little of mathematics. So let these definitions be taken as an explanation of the name. For if proof establishes that what they explained is as they explained it, it is believed to be a definition; otherwise it is believed to be an explanation of the name, as we say: the definition of jinn is an airy, speaking, transparent animal whose property is to assume various forms; so this is an explanation of the name in people's understanding. As for the existence of the thing in this manner, it is known by proof. If proof indicates its existence, it is a definition according to the essence; if it does not indicate it but rather indicates that the jinn intended in the Law described by that description is something else, then this is taken as an explanation of the name in people's understanding. And as we say in the definition of void: it is a dimension in which three dimensions can be supposed, a body standing not in matter, and perhaps the evidence indicates that this is impossible from the body that is such as to fill it and be empty of it. We have presented this introduction so that you know that what we present of the definitions is an explanation of what the philosophers intended in their usage, not a judgment that what they mentioned is as they mentioned it, for that perhaps depends on considering the requirement of proof upon it.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the second art of logic, which deals with detailed definitions. The author begins by noting that the number of definable things is infinite, because assenting sciences are infinite and depend on conceptual sciences. Therefore, one cannot cover everything, so the first art provided the general rules. The author then states that he presents detailed definitions for two purposes: first, to give the student practice in formulating definitions, since practice strengthens ability; second, to explain the technical terms used by philosophers, which is necessary for debating them. These definitions are to be taken as explanations of names (sharh al-ism), not necessarily as real definitions (hadd) unless proof confirms that the definition matches reality. The author gives two examples: the definition of jinn as an airy, speaking, transparent animal that can assume forms, which is merely an explanation of the name used in common parlance; and the definition of void as a dimension in which three dimensions can be supposed, which may be proven impossible by evidence. The key point is that these definitions are not asserted as true; they are only explanations of what the philosophers mean. Their truth depends on demonstration.

Why God Cannot Be Defined: The Case of al-Bari
Defining al-Bari as the Necessary Existent beyond definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the philosophers deny that al-Bari can have a proper definition (hadd) or description (rasm), and identify the absent components (genus, differentia, accidents).

Core Idea
God is beyond genus and differentia, so real definitions cannot capture Him.

In this lesson, we examine a crucial application of the theory of definition from Islamic logic: the case of al-Bari, the Creator. The philosophers argue that al-Bari cannot be defined by hadd or rasm because He has no genus, differentia, or accidents. Instead, they offer only a verbal explanation (sharh al-ism). Understanding this deepens your grasp of the limits of definition and the nature of God in philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Recall the components of hadd and rasm
A real definition (hadd) consists of genus (jins) and differentia (fasl); a description (rasm) consists of genus and differentiating accidents (al-'awarid al-fasila). These components impose composition.
2
Identify why they do not apply to al-Bari
The passage states clearly: 'la jinsa lahu wa la fasla lahu wa la 'awarida talhaquhu' – He has no genus, no differentia, and no accidents. Therefore, no hadd or rasm is possible.
3
Understand the alternative: sharh al-ism
Instead of a definition, the philosophers give a verbal explanation of the name 'al-Bari': He is the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud), whose existence is from Himself, and all existence flows from Him. This is not a definition but a nominal explanation.
4
Analyze the negations implied
The explanation entails negation of multiplicity – in number, quantity, parts of subsistence (like matter and form), parts of definition (like genus and differentia), and parts of relation. It also negates attributes, which is a point of contention with Islamic orthodoxy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمستعمل في الإلهيات خمسة عشر لفظاً وهي: الباري تعالى المسمى بلسانهم المبدأ الأول والعقل والنفس، والعقل الكلي، وعقل الكل، والنفس الكلية، ونفس الكل، والملك والعلة والمعلول، والإبداع، والخلق، والإحداث والقديم. أما الباري عز وجل فزعموا أنه لا حد له ولا رسم له، لأنه لا جنس له ولا فصل له ولا عوارض تلحقه، والحد يلتئم بالجنس والفصل، والرسم بالجنس والعوارض الفاصلة، وكل ذلك تركيب، ولكن له قول يشرح اسمه، وهو أنه الموجود الواجب الوجود الذي لا يمكن أن يكون وجوده من غيره ولا يكون وجود لسواه إلا فائضاً عن وجوده وحاصلاً به إما بواسطة أو بغير واسطة، ويتبع هذا الشرح أنه الموجود الذي لا يتكثر لا بالعدد ولا بالمقدار ولا بأجزاء القوام كتكثر الجسم بالصورة والهيولى، ولا بأجزاء الحد كتكثر الإنسان بالحيوانية والنطق، ولا بأجزاء الإضافة ولا يتغير لا في الذات ولا في لواحق الذات، وما ذكروه يشتمل على نفي الصفات ونفي الكثرة فيها، وذلك مما يخالفون فيه، فهذا شرح اسم الباري والمبدأ الأول عندهم.
And the terms used in metaphysics are fifteen: al-Bari the Exalted, called in their language the First Principle, Intellect, Soul, Universal Intellect, Intellect of the Whole, Universal Soul, Soul of the Whole, Angel, Cause, Effect, Origination, Creation, Invention, and the Eternal. As for al-Bari, may He be glorified and exalted, they claim that He has no definition nor description, because He has no genus, no differentia, and no accidents that attach to Him. The definition is composed of genus and differentia, and the description of genus and differentiating accidents – all that is composition. But He has a statement that explains His name, namely that He is the Necessary Existent whose existence cannot be from another, and no existence exists except as flowing from His existence and attained through Him, either directly or indirectly. This explanation entails that He is the Existent who is not multiplied by number, quantity, or parts of subsistence like the multiplication of a body by form and matter, nor by parts of definition like the multiplication of human by animality and rationality, nor by parts of relation, and He does not change in essence or in the concomitants of essence. What they have mentioned includes negation of attributes and negation of multiplicity in them, and that is something in which they oppose (the truth). So this is the explanation of the name 'al-Bari' and 'the First Principle' according to them.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-Bari' (the Creator) as used by the philosophers in metaphysics. The author begins by listing fifteen terms used in ilahiyat, including al-Bari, Intellect, Soul, etc. Then he focuses on al-Bari, explaining that according to the philosophers, He cannot have a proper definition (hadd) or description (rasm) because He has no genus, differentia, or accidents – the components of definition. Instead, they provide a verbal explanation (sharh al-ism) of His name: He is the Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) whose existence is from Himself, and all other existence flows from Him. The author then elaborates the implications: al-Bari is free from all multiplicity – not multiplied in number, quantity, parts like matter and form, or definitional parts like genus and differentia. This is a theological issue where the philosophers differ from Islamic orthodoxy by negating attributes. The passage thus presents the philosophers' view on the unspeakable nature of God and the need for a nominal 'explanation' rather than a real definition.

Key Terms
الباري
الحد
الرسم
الواجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because definitions are only for material things.
D) Because they reject the concept of definition.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers hold that God cannot be defined by genus and differentia because He is beyond composition; only a negative explanation is possible.

The Three Common Meanings of 'Aql
Author enumerates three common usages of 'aql with definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three common meanings of 'aql as used in everyday language and identify the source of disputes arising from homonymy.

Core Idea
The word 'aql has three distinct but overlapping meanings, causing confusion when used imprecisely.

In everyday language, the term 'aql (intellect) is used in multiple ways. The author of this passage identifies three common usages: as a faculty of discrimination, as experiential knowledge, and as dignified composure. Understanding these distinctions is crucial before moving to technical definitions in logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three definitions
Locate each definition in the Arabic text and note the core feature: first meaning is a faculty for distinguishing good from bad; second is universal judgments from experience; third is dignified demeanor.
The Arabic uses 'qawwa' for faculty and 'hay'a' for disposition.
2
Compare the genus of each
The first genus is a power, the second is acquired knowledge, the third is a personal quality. They are fundamentally different types of entities.
3
Recognize the homonymy cause
The same word 'aql is used for different meanings, leading people to disagree when calling someone 'intelligent' based on different criteria.
The passage explicitly gives an example of such a dispute.
4
Link to technical precision
In Islamic theology and law, a precise definition of 'aql is needed to avoid equivocation in arguments about moral responsibility and knowledge.
This prepares you for later technical definitions in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى : إنه عاقل، فيكون حدّه أنه قوة بها يجود التمييز بين الأمور القبيحة والحسنة . الثاني : يراد به ما يكتسبه الإنسان بالتجارب من الأحكام الكلية، فيكون حدّه أنه معانٍ مجتمعة في الذهن تكون مقدمات يستنبط بها المصالح والأغراض . الثالث : معنى آخر يرجع إلى وقار الإنسان وهيئته، ويكون حده أنه هيئة محمودة للإنسان في حركاته وسكناته وهيئاته وكلامه واختياره، ولهذا الاشتراك يتنازع الناس في تسمية الشخص الواحد عاقلاً فيقول واحد: هذا عاقل، ويعني به صحة الغريزة، ويقول الآخر: ليس بعاقل ويعني به عدم التجارب وهو المعنى الثاني .
The first: he is intelligent, so its definition is that it is a faculty by which one excels in distinguishing between ugly and good matters. The second: by it is meant what a person acquires through experiences of universal judgments, so its definition is that it is meanings gathered in the mind that serve as premises from which benefits and purposes are derived. The third: another meaning referring to a person's dignity and demeanor, and its definition is that it is a praiseworthy disposition in a person's movements, stillness, states, speech, and choice. Due to this homonymy, people dispute about calling one person intelligent: one says 'this is intelligent', meaning by it sound instinct, and another says 'he is not intelligent', meaning by it lack of experience, which is the second meaning.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents three common meanings of the word 'aql (intellect/reason) as used among the general populace. The first meaning defines the intelligent person as one who possesses a faculty enabling excellent discrimination between good and bad actions—this is the basic rational instinct. The second meaning refers to the universal judgments acquired through experience, forming a collection of premises in the mind from which one derives benefits and purposes. The third meaning relates to dignified composure, a praiseworthy demeanor in movement, speech, and choices. The author notes that due to this homonymy, disagreements arise: one may call a person 'intelligent' based on sound instinct, while another denies it due to lack of experiential knowledge, which is the second meaning. This sets the stage for the need for technical precision in philosophical and theological discussions.

قوة

C) The ability to calculate mathematical equations
D) A praiseworthy disposition in demeanor and speech

Key Takeaway
The term 'aql is homonymous; precise usage requires specifying whether one means the innate faculty, experiential knowledge, or dignified demeanor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Eight Meanings of 'Aql According to the Philosophers
Author lists eight philosophical meanings of 'aql.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and distinguish the eight philosophical meanings of 'aql from memory.

Core Idea
'Aql is a homonym with eight distinct meanings – knowing them prevents confusion in theological debate.

The philosophers (falasifa) used the term 'aql (intellect) in multiple ways. Recognizing these distinct meanings is essential for understanding their discussions on epistemology and psychology. In this lesson, we will enumerate the eight senses they assigned to 'aql, as given in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Recognize homonymy of 'aql
Find where the author says the name 'aql is 'mushtarak' (homonymous) and understand that it has multiple, distinct meanings in philosophical usage.
Homonymy is common in technical terms; a single term can refer to different concepts.
2
Extract the eight meanings from the text
Carefully read the Arabic list: العقل الذي يريده المتكلمون، العقل النظري، العقل العملي، العقل الهيولاني، العقل بالملكة، العقل بالفعل، العقل المستفاد، العقل الفعال. Write them down in order.
The first meaning is the one intended by the theologians; the rest are from the Book of the Soul.
3
Group the meanings by source or function
Note that the first meaning is discussed separately in the Book of Demonstration (كتاب البرهان), while the others are treated in the Book of the Soul (كتاب النفس). Also, the theoretical and practical intellects form a pair, and the hylic, habitual, actual, acquired, and active intellects represent stages of intellectual actualization.
This grouping helps in memorization and understanding the conceptual domains.
4
Apply the classification to an example
Consider a statement like 'aql is necessary knowledge' – which meaning does it refer to? The first (theologians' intellect) because it is innate and necessary.
Identifying the correct meaning avoids misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فاسم العقل عندهم مشترك يدلّ على ثمانية معان مختلفة : العقل الذي يريده المتكلمون والعقل النظري والعقل العملي والعقل الهيولاني، والعقل بالملكة والعقل بالفعل، والعقل المُستفاد والعقل الفعّال . فأما الأول : فهو الذي ذكره أرسطاليس في كتاب البرهان وفرّق بينه وبين العلم، ومعنى هذا العقل هو التصوّرات والتصديقات الحاصلة للنفس بالفطرة، والعلم ما يحصل للنفس بالاكتساب، ففرقوا بين المكتسب والفطري فيسمى أحدهما عقلاً والآخر علماً، وهو اصطلاح محض. وهذا المعنى هو الذي حدّ المتكلمون العقل به إذ قال القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني في حد العقل : إنه علم ضروري بجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات كالعلم باستحالة كون الشيء الواحد قديماً وحديثاً واستحالة كون الشخص الواحد في مكانين. وأما سائر العقول فذكرها الفلاسفة في كتاب النفس .
As for the philosophers, the name 'aql is homonymous for them, indicating eight different meanings: the intellect intended by the theologians, the theoretical intellect, the practical intellect, the hylic intellect, the intellect in habitu, the actual intellect, the acquired intellect, and the active intellect. As for the first: it is that which Aristotle mentioned in the Book of Demonstration, and he distinguished between it and 'ilm. The meaning of this 'aql is the conceptualizations and assents that occur to the soul by innate disposition, while 'ilm is what occurs to the soul through acquisition. So they distinguished between the acquired and the innate, calling one 'aql and the other 'ilm, and this is a pure convention. And this meaning is that by which the theologians defined 'aql, when al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said in the definition of 'aql: 'It is necessary knowledge of the possibility of possible things and the impossibility of impossible things, such as the knowledge of the impossibility of something being both old and new, and the impossibility of a single person being in two places.' As for the rest of the intellects, the philosophers have mentioned them in the Book of the Soul.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage introduces the philosophers' classification of 'aql into eight distinct meanings. The author first lists them: the intellect of the theologians, theoretical, practical, hylic, habitual, actual, acquired, and active. He then elaborates on the first—the intellect of the theologians—which is the innate conceptualizations and assents (tasawwurat and tasdiqat) in the soul, as distinguished from acquired knowledge ('ilm). This distinction between innate and acquired is purely conventional. The author cites al-Baqillani's definition: 'aql is necessary knowledge of what is possible and impossible, e.g., knowing that one thing cannot be both old and new. This aligns with the theologians' usage. The other intellects are treated in works on the soul, not detailed here.

Key Takeaway

The First Meaning: Innate 'Aql vs. Acquired 'Ilm
Author expounds the first meaning of 'aql and cites al-Baqillani's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the distinction between 'aql and 'ilm and reproduce al-Baqillani's definition of 'aql.

Core Idea
Theologians define 'aql as innate necessary knowledge of possibilities and impossibilities — it is not acquired.

The first meaning of 'aql is the one used by theologians (mutakallimun). Philosophers distinguish it from acquired knowledge ('ilm). This lesson unpacks that distinction and the precise definition given by al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, a foundational figure in Ash'ari kalam.

Guided Path
1
Locate the first meaning in the passage
Find where the author says 'فأما الأول' (As for the first) and note that it refers to the intellect intended by the theologians.
This is the only meaning discussed in detail here.
2
Extract the distinction between 'aql and 'ilm
Read: 'aql is the conceptualizations and assents (tasawwurat wa tasdiqat) that occur to the soul by innate disposition (bil-fitrah); 'ilm is what occurs through acquisition (bil-iktisab). Write this contrast.
The distinction is purely conventional (istilah mahd).
3
Analyze al-Baqillani's definition
Identify the definition: 'necessary knowledge of the possibility of possible things and the impossibility of impossible things' (علم ضروري بجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات). Break it down: genus = necessary knowledge, differentia = of possibilities and impossibilities.
The examples given are: impossibility of one thing being both old and new, and impossibility of one person being in two places.
4
Test the definition with an example
Consider the proposition 'A triangle has three sides'. Is this 'aql or 'ilm? It is innate necessary knowledge (once terms are understood), so it is 'aql. Contrast with 'The sum of angles in a triangle is 180 degrees' — that is acquired through reasoning, hence 'ilm.
This helps solidify the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فاسم العقل عندهم مشترك يدلّ على ثمانية معان مختلفة : العقل الذي يريده المتكلمون والعقل النظري والعقل العملي والعقل الهيولاني، والعقل بالملكة والعقل بالفعل، والعقل المُستفاد والعقل الفعّال . فأما الأول : فهو الذي ذكره أرسطاليس في كتاب البرهان وفرّق بينه وبين العلم، ومعنى هذا العقل هو التصوّرات والتصديقات الحاصلة للنفس بالفطرة، والعلم ما يحصل للنفس بالاكتساب، ففرقوا بين المكتسب والفطري فيسمى أحدهما عقلاً والآخر علماً، وهو اصطلاح محض. وهذا المعنى هو الذي حدّ المتكلمون العقل به إذ قال القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني في حد العقل : إنه علم ضروري بجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات كالعلم باستحالة كون الشيء الواحد قديماً وحديثاً واستحالة كون الشخص الواحد في مكانين. وأما سائر العقول فذكرها الفلاسفة في كتاب النفس .
As for the philosophers, the name 'aql is homonymous for them, indicating eight different meanings: the intellect intended by the theologians, the theoretical intellect, the practical intellect, the hylic intellect, the intellect in habitu, the actual intellect, the acquired intellect, and the active intellect. As for the first: it is that which Aristotle mentioned in the Book of Demonstration, and he distinguished between it and 'ilm. The meaning of this 'aql is the conceptualizations and assents that occur to the soul by innate disposition, while 'ilm is what occurs to the soul through acquisition. So they distinguished between the acquired and the innate, calling one 'aql and the other 'ilm, and this is a pure convention. And this meaning is that by which the theologians defined 'aql, when al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said in the definition of 'aql: 'It is necessary knowledge of the possibility of possible things and the impossibility of impossible things, such as the knowledge of the impossibility of something being both old and new, and the impossibility of a single person being in two places.' As for the rest of the intellects, the philosophers have mentioned them in the Book of the Soul.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage introduces the philosophers' classification of 'aql into eight distinct meanings. The author first lists them: the intellect of the theologians, theoretical, practical, hylic, habitual, actual, acquired, and active. He then elaborates on the first—the intellect of the theologians—which is the innate conceptualizations and assents (tasawwurat and tasdiqat) in the soul, as distinguished from acquired knowledge ('ilm). This distinction between innate and acquired is purely conventional. Theoretical vs. Practical Intellect
Author defines theoretical and practical intellect and contrasts them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between theoretical and practical intellect and identify their roles in Islamic psychology.

Core Idea
Theoretical intellect knows universals, practical intellect moves to action.

The author defines two intellectual faculties: one that grasps universal truths, and another that drives action. Understanding their difference is crucial for appreciating why knowledge does not always translate into practice—a key insight in Islamic spirituality and ethics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of theoretical intellect
Find the sentence starting with 'As for the theoretical intellect'. Notice that it is described as a faculty that receives universal quiddities as universals, distinguishing it from sense and imagination which deal with particulars.
The phrase 'as universals' is key to its nature.
2
Identify the definition of practical intellect
Locate the definition of practical intellect. It is a faculty that moves the appetitive power toward chosen particulars for a goal. Note that it is explicitly called a 'moving faculty, not of the genus of sciences'.
The practical intellect is not a kind of knowledge but an executive power.
3
Analyze the example of the weak-willed person
Read the example: a person knows that following desires harms him but cannot oppose them. The author says the deficiency is not in theoretical intellect but in practical intellect. This illustrates the gap between knowing and acting.
This example mirrors the Islamic concept of mujahada (striving against the nafs).
4
Determine how practical intellect is strengthened
The passage states that the practical intellect is strengthened by discipline, striving, and persistence in opposing desires. This connects to Islamic spiritual practices like riyada and mujahada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العقل النظري فهي قوة للنفس تقبل ماهيات الأمور الكلية كلية، وهي احتراز عن الحس الذي لا يقبل إلَّا الأمور الجزئية وكذا الخيال، وكأن المراد بصحة الفطرة الأصلية عند الجماهير كما سبق . وأما العقل العملي فقوة للنفس هي مبدأ التحريك للقوة الشوقية إلى ما تختاره الجزئيات، لأجل غاية مظنونة أو معلومة، وهذه قوة محركة ليس من جنس العلوم، وإنما سمّيت عقلية لأنها مؤتمرة للعقل مطيعة لإشاراته بالطبع، فكم من عاقل يعرف أنه مُستضرُّ باتباع شهواته، ولكنه يعجز عن المخالفة للشهوة لا لقصور في عقله النظري بل لفتور هذه القوة التي سمّيت العقل العملي، وإنما تقوى هذه القوة بالرياضة والمجاهدة والمواظبة على مخالفة الشهوات .
As for the theoretical intellect, it is a faculty of the soul that receives the quiddities of universal things as universals—a precaution against sense perception, which only receives particulars, and likewise imagination. And it seems that what is meant by sound original instinct among the masses is as previously mentioned. As for the practical intellect, it is a faculty of the soul that is the principle of moving the appetitive faculty toward what it chooses of particulars, for the sake of a conjectured or known goal. This is a moving faculty, not of the genus of sciences; it is called 'intellectual' only because it follows the intellect and obeys its indications by nature. How many an intelligent person knows that he is harmed by following his desires, but he is unable to oppose the desire—not due to a deficiency in his theoretical intellect, but due to the weakness of this faculty called the practical intellect. And this faculty is strengthened only through discipline, striving, and persistence in opposing desires.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage defines the theoretical and practical intellects. The theoretical intellect (al-'aql al-nazari) is a faculty that receives universal quiddities as universals, distinguishing it from sense perception (which perceives only particulars) and imagination (which also deals with particulars). It corresponds to the sound instinct mentioned earlier. The practical intellect (al-'aql al-'amali) is a faculty that moves the appetitive power (al-quwwa al-shawqiyya) toward chosen particular actions for a goal, whether known or conjectured. Although it is a moving faculty and not a science, it is called 'intellectual' because it obeys the intellect's indications by nature. The author illustrates the distinction: a person may have a sound theoretical intellect, knowing that following desires harms him, yet fail to act due to weakness in the practical intellect. This faculty is strengthened by spiritual discipline (riyada) and persistent struggle against desires. This relates to the Islamic concept of mujahada.

Key Terms
العقل النظري
العقل العملي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because it obeys the intellect's indications by nature
C) Because it is the same as theoretical intellect
D) Because it acquires universal quiddities

Key Takeaway
Theoretical intellect knows universals; practical intellect moves toward action and is strengthened by discipline.

?
