The Four States of Theoretical Intellect
Author enumerates and defines the four developmental stages of theoretical intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the four states of theoretical intellect and match each to its formal definition.

Core Idea
Intellect progresses from pure potentiality to active contemplation through four defined stages.

This passage presents a foundational taxonomy in Islamic philosophy: the four states of the theoretical intellect. Understanding these stages—from the infant's blank slate to the scholar's active knowledge—is essential for grasping how humans come to know universal truths. The author first enumerates the states descriptively, then provides formal definitions (ḥudūd) for each. This lesson will help you internalize the progression and recognize each state's defining characteristics.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four states
Read the Arabic from the beginning: 'ثم للقوة النظرية أربعة أحوال : الأولى... الثانية... الثالثة... الرابعة...'. Identify the four numbered states in order.
The states are explicitly numbered in the text.
2
Identify the defining feature of each state
For each state, note the key characteristic: hylic intellect lacks all knowledge; intellect in habitu has proximate potentiality for necessary truths; actual intellect possesses forms but is heedless; acquired intellect actively contemplates.
The author gives both a descriptive account and a formal definition later.
3
Compare the formal definitions
Find the section starting with 'فحدّ العقل الهيولاني...' Read each definition and note its genus and differentia. For example, hylic intellect is defined as 'a faculty of the soul prepared to receive quiddities abstracted from matter'.
The definitions use terms like qūwa, isti'dād, istikmāl, māhiyya.
4
Distinguish remote and proximate potentiality
Notice that hylic intellect is remote potentiality (bil-quwwa al-ba'īda), while intellect in habitu is proximate potentiality (bil-quwwa al-qarība). This distinction is crucial for understanding the progression.
The infant (ṣabī ṣaghīr) has remote potentiality; the child at the age of discrimination (tamyīz) has proximate potentiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم للقوة النظرية أربعة أحوال : الأولى : أن لا يكون لها شيء من المعلومات حاصلة، وذلك للصبي الصغير، ولكن فيه مجرّد الاستعداد فَيُسمى هذا عقلاً هيولانياً . الثانية : أن ينتهي الصبي إلى حدّ التمييز فيصير ما كان بالقوة البعيدة بالقوة القريبة، فإنه مهما عرض عليه الضروريات وجد نفسه مصدّقاً بها. لا كالصبي الذي هو ابن مهد، وهذا يُسمّى العقل بالملكة . الثالثة : أن تكون المعقولات النظرية حاصلة في ذهنه، ولكنه غافل عنها ولكن متى شاء أحضرها بالفعل، ويُسمّى عقلاً بالفعل . الرابعة : العقل المستفاد وهو أن تكون تلك المعلومات حاضرة في ذهنه وهو يطالعها ويلابس التأمل فيها، وهو العلم الموجود بالفعل الحاضر؛ فحدّ العقل الهيولاني أنه قوة للنفس مُستعدة لقبول ماهيّات الأشياء مجرّدة عن المواد، وبها يفارق الصبي الفرس وسائر الحيوانات لا يعلم حاضر ولا بقوة قريبة من العلم، وحدّ العقل بالملكة أنه استكمال العقل الهيولاني حتى يصير بالقوة القريبة من الفعل، وحدّ العقل بالفعل أنه استكمال للنفس بصور ما أي صور معقولة حتى شاء عقلها أو أحضرها بالفعل، وحدّ العقل المستفاد أنه ماهية مجرَّدة عن المادة مرتسمة في النفس على سبيل الحصول من خارج .
Then for the theoretical faculty there are four states: The first: that it has none of the items of information present, as with a small child, but in him there is mere potentiality, so this is called 'hylic intellect'. The second: that the child reaches the age of discrimination, so that what was in remote potentiality becomes in proximate potentiality, for whenever necessary truths are presented to him, he finds himself assenting to them—unlike the child who is an infant in the cradle—and this is called 'intellect in habitu'. The third: that theoretical intelligibles are present in his mind, but he is heedless of them, yet whenever he wishes he brings them to actuality, and it is called 'actual intellect'. The fourth: the acquired intellect, which is that those pieces of information are present in his mind while he is studying them and engaged in contemplating them, and it is knowledge existing in actuality as present. So the definition of hylic intellect is that it is a faculty of the soul prepared to receive the quiddities of things abstracted from matter, and by it a child differs from a horse and other animals—he knows neither what is present nor in proximate potentiality to knowledge. The definition of intellect in habitu is that it is the perfection of the hylic intellect until it becomes in proximate potentiality to act. The definition of actual intellect is that it is a perfection of the soul with forms—i.e., intelligible forms—such that whenever he wills, he intellects them or brings them to actuality. The definition of acquired intellect is that it is a quiddity abstracted from matter, impressed in the soul as acquired from outside.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage details the four developmental states of the theoretical faculty (al-quwwa al-nazariyya). The first state, hylic intellect (al-'aql al-hayulani), is pure potentiality with no knowledge actualized, as in a young child—only mere disposition. The second, intellect in habitu (al-'aql bi-l-malaka), occurs when the child reaches the age of discrimination (tamyiz) so that necessary truths are immediately assented to upon presentation; unlike an infant in the cradle, he has proximate potentiality. The third, actual intellect (al-'aql bi-l-fi'l), is when intelligibles are present in the mind but not currently being attended to, yet can be brought to actuality at will. The fourth, acquired intellect (al-'aql al-mustafad), is when those intelligibles are actually present and being contemplated—active knowledge. The author then gives formal definitions for each: hylic intellect is the receptive faculty, differentiating humans from animals; intellect in habitu is its perfection to proximate potentiality; actual intellect is the soul's perfection with intelligible forms; acquired intellect is the abstract quiddity impressed from outside. This is a standard Peripatetic classification of the intellect's stages.

Key Terms
العقل الهيولاني
العقل بالملكة
العقل بالفعل
العقل المستفاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which state of theoretical intellect is defined as 'the perfection of the hylic intellect until it becomes in proximate potentiality to act'?
A) Hylic intellect
B) Intellect in habitu
C) Actual intellect
D) Acquired intellect

Key Takeaway
The theoretical intellect progresses from pure potentiality (hylic) through dispositional readiness (in habitu) to possession of forms (actual) and finally to active contemplation (acquired).

The Twofold Definition of the Active Intellect
Defines the active intellect from two perspectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two perspectives from which the Peripatetics define the active intellect and identify the genus and differentiae in each.

Core Idea
The active intellect is defined both by its abstract essence and its causal role.

This lesson unpacks the Peripatetic definition of the active intellect as presented in the source. The definition proceeds from two complementary angles: its nature as an intellect and its function as active. Understanding this twofold definition is essential for grasping the philosophers' cosmology and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitional phrases
Find the sentences beginning with 'أما من جهة ما هو عقل' and 'أما من جهة ما هو فعّال'. These introduce the two aspects of the definition.
2
Extract the genus
Both definitions call the active intellect a 'jawhar' (substance). Later, the author clarifies that jawhar here means self-subsistent, not extended.
3
Identify the differentia for the intellect aspect
As intellect, the differentia is being 'abstract in itself, with a universal quiddity, not abstracted by another.' This excludes mental abstractions from material particulars.
4
Identify the differentia for the active aspect
As active, the differentia is its function: bringing the hylic intellect from potentiality to actuality by illumination.
5
Verify the definition's completeness
Check that the definition excludes bodies (by 'formal'), what is in matter, and intelligibles abstracted by the mind (by 'not by abstraction of another'). It covers only the active intellect itself.
The author states this is an explanation of the name, not a full demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فحدّ العقل الفعّال أما من جهة ما هو عقل أنه ما هيَّة مجرّدة في ذاتها، لا بتجريد غيرها لها ماهية كلية موجودة، فأما ذاته جوهر صوري عن المادة وعن علائق المادة، بل هي من جهة ما هو فعّال فإنه جوهر بالصفة المذكورة من شأنه أن يخرج العقل الهيولاني من القوة إلى الفعل بإشراقه عليه، وليس المراد بالجوهر المتحيّز كما يريده المتلكمون ، بل ما هو قائم بنفسه لا في موضوع، والصوري احتراز عن الجسم وما في المواد وقولهم : لا بتجريد غيره احتراز عن المعقولات المرتسمة في النفس من أشخاص الماديات، فإنها مجردة بتجريد العقل إياها لا بتجرّدها في ذاتها. والعقل الفعّال المخرج لنفوس الآدميين في العلوم من القوة إلى الفعل نسبته إلى المعقولات والقوة العاقلة نسبة الشمس إلى المبصرات والقوة الباصرة، إذ بها يخرج الإبصار من القوة إلى الفعل، وقد يسمون هذه العقول الملائكة، وفي وجود جوهر على هذا الوجه يخالفهم متكلمون، إذ لا وجود لقائم بنفسه ليس بمتحيّز عندهم إلا الله وحده. والملائكة أجسام لطيفة متحيّزة عند أكثرهم، وتصحيح ذلك بطريق البرهان وما ذكرناه شرح الاسم.
As for the definition of the active intellect: from the perspective of being intellect, it is an essence abstracted in itself, not by the abstraction of another, having a universal existing quiddity. As for its essence, it is a formal substance free from matter and material attachments. From the perspective of being active, it is a substance with the mentioned attribute whose function is to bring the hylic intellect from potentiality to actuality by its illumination upon it. The intended meaning of substance is not the extended substance as the mutakallimun hold, but rather that which is self-subsistent not in a subject. 'Formal' excludes body and what is in matter. Their statement 'not by the abstraction of another' excludes the intelligibles imprinted in the soul from individual material things, for they are abstracted by the intellect's abstraction, not by their own abstraction. The active intellect, which brings human souls in sciences from potentiality to actuality, its relation to intelligibles and the rational power is like the sun's relation to visible things and the visual power, for by it vision is brought from potentiality to actuality. They sometimes call these intellects angels. As for the existence of a substance in this manner, the mutakallimun oppose them, for in their view there is no self-subsistent non-extended existence except God alone. Angels are subtle extended bodies according to most of them. The verification of that is by way of demonstration, and what we have mentioned is an explanation of the name.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage defines the active intellect ('aql fa''al) as understood by the Peripatetic philosophers. The definition proceeds from two angles: first, what it is as an intellect ('aql), and second, what it is as active (fa''al). As an intellect, it is an essence that is abstracted in itself, not abstracted by an external agent, and it has a universal quiddity that exists. As an intellect, it is a formal substance (jawhar suri) free from matter and material attachments. The term 'formal' (suri) distinguishes it from a body and from things that exist in matter. As active, it is the substance that brings the hylic intellect (al-'aql al-hayulani) from potentiality (quwwa) to actuality (fi'l) by its illumination. The author clarifies that the philosophers do not intend by 'substance' (jawhar) the extended (mutahayyiz) substance as the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) hold; rather, they mean that which is self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsihi) not in a subject. The phrase 'not by the abstraction of another' excludes the intelligibles that are imprinted in the soul from material particulars; those are abstracted by the intellect's act, not by themselves. The active intellect's function in bringing human souls to knowledge is likened to the sun's role in bringing vision from potentiality to actuality. The philosophers sometimes call these intellects 'angels' (mala'ika). The mutakallimun, however, deny any self-subsistent non-extended entity except God; angels, in their view, are subtle extended bodies. The author notes that the demonstration of this is by way of proof, and the above is merely an explanation of the name.

Key Terms
العقل الفعّال
الجوهر
الصوري
العقل الهيولاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is abstracted by another.
B) It is abstract in itself.
C) It is material.
D) It is extended.

Key Takeaway
The active intellect is a self-subsistent, non-extended formal substance defined by both its abstract nature and its causative role in actualizing human intellect.

Substance (Jawhar) and the Mutakallimun's Objection
Contrasts philosophical and kalam definitions of substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the philosophers' definition of jawhar (as self-subsistent) with the mutakallimun's definition (as extended) and explain the theological implication regarding angels.

Core Idea
The definition of substance is contested: extension is not a condition for philosophers but is for mutakallimun.

This lesson focuses on the clarification of the term 'jawhar' in the passage. The author explicitly distinguishes the philosophical meaning from the kalam meaning. Understanding this terminological shift is crucial for avoiding category errors when studying Islamic philosophy and theology.

Guided Path
1
Find where the author clarifies 'jawhar'
Locate the statement 'وليس المراد بالجوهر المتحيز كما يريده المتكلمون'. This explicitly contrasts the two definitions.
2
State the philosophers' definition
Jawhar = qa'im bi-nafsihi la fi mawdu' (self-subsistent, not in a subject).
3
State the mutakallimun's definition
Jawhar = mutahayyiz (extended, occupying space).
4
Identify the theological consequence
For mutakallimun, only God is non-extended and self-subsistent; angels are subtle extended bodies. Philosophers allow multiple non-extended substances like the active intellect.
5
Assess the purpose of the contrast
The author is noting a point of disagreement to preempt objections from the mutakallimun against the existence of such a substance.

Source Text

As for the definition of the active intellect: from the perspective of being intellect, it is an essence abstracted in itself, not by the abstraction of another, having a universal existing quiddity. As for its essence, it is a formal substance free from matter and material attachments. From the perspective of being active, it is a substance with the mentioned attribute whose function is to bring the hylic intellect from potentiality to actuality by its illumination upon it. They sometimes call these intellects angels. This passage defines the active intellect ('aql fa''al) as understood by the Peripatetic philosophers. The definition proceeds from two angles: first, what it is as an intellect ('aql), and second, what it is as active (fa''al). Anatomy of the First Definition of the Soul
Defines the soul's first meaning with logical components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentiae in the first definition of the soul and explain why 'perfection' is chosen as the genus.

Core Idea
The soul's first definition uses 'perfection' as genus because it captures both form and power.

The passage defines the soul as a first perfection of a natural organic body with potential life. This is a classic logical definition (hadd). In this lesson, we will dissect each part: the genus (first perfection) and the differentiae (natural, organic, having life potentially). We'll also see why 'perfection' is a better genus than 'form' or 'power'.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the exact phrase in the Arabic: 'كمال جسم طبيعي آلي ذي حياة بالقوة' (a first perfection of a natural organic body having life potentially). This is the hadd being analyzed.
2
Identify the genus
The genus is 'كمال أول' (first perfection). The text explains that 'perfection' is placed in the position of the genus because its signification is more complete than 'form' or 'power'.
Consider why 'perfection' encompasses both form and power.
3
Extract the differentiae
The differentiae are: 'طبيعي' (natural) to exclude artificial bodies, 'آلي' (organic) to exclude elemental forces, and 'ذي حياة بالقوة' (having life potentially) to specify that the soul is the principle of life functions, not yet actual operations.
Each differentia narrows down the genus to exactly the soul.
4
Explain the choice of 'perfection' over 'form' or 'power'
The text states: 'ودلالة الكمال أتم من دلالة القوة والصورة' (the signification of perfection is more complete than that of power and form). Therefore, it is better suited as the genus for a definition that covers vegetative, animal, and rational souls.
This logical choice ensures the definition is neither too narrow nor too broad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفس فهو عندهم اسم مشترك يقع على معنى يشترك فيه الإنسان والحيوان والنبات وعلى معنى آخر يشترك فيه الإنسان والملائكة السماوية عندهم، فحدّ النفس بالمعنى الأول عندهم أنه كمال جسم طبيعي آلي ذي حياة بالقوة، وحدّ النفس بالمعنى الآخر أنه جوهر غير جسم هو كمال أول للجسم محرك له بالاختيار عن مبدأ نطقي أي عقلي بالفعل أو بالقوة، فالذي بالقوة هو فصل النفس الإنسانية، والذي بالفعل هو فصل أو خاصة للنفس الملكية. وشرح الحد الأول أن حبّة البذر إذا طُرحت في الأرض، فاستعدت للنمو والاغتذاء فقد تغيّرت عما كان عليه قبل طرحه في الأرض، وذلك بحدوث صفة فيه لو لم تكن . لما استعد لقبولها من واهب الصور، وهو الله تعالى وملائكته، فتلك الصفة كمال له فلذلك قيل في الحدّ إنه كمال أول للجسم، ووضع ذلك موضع الجنس، وهذا يشترك فيه البذر والنطفة للحيوان والإنسان. فالنفس صورة بالقياس إلى المادة الممتزجة، إذ هي منطبعة في المادة وهي قوة بالقياس إلى فعلها. وكمال بالقياس إلى النوع النباتي والحيواني ودلالة الكمال أتم من دلالة القوة والصورة، فلذلك عبر به في محلّ الجنس والطبيعي احتراز عن الصناعي فإن صور الصناعات أيضاً كمال فيها، والآلي احتراز عن القوى التي في العناصر الأربعة، فإنها تفعل لا بآلات بل بذواتها، والقوى النفسانية فعلها بآلات فيها وقولهم «ذو حياة بالقوة» فصل آخر أي من شأنه أن يحيا بالنشوء ويبقى بالغذاء، وربما يحيا بإحساس وحركة هما في قوته. وقولهم: «كمال أول الاحتراز بالأول عـن قـوة الـتـحـريـك والإحساس، فإنه أيضاً كمال للجسم لكنه ليس كمالاً أولاً يقع ثانياً لوجود الكمال الذي هو نفس.
As for the soul, it is in their view a homonym that applies to a meaning shared by man, animal, and plant, and to another meaning shared by man and the celestial angels in their view. The definition of the soul in the first meaning in their view is: a first perfection of a natural organic body having life potentially. The definition of the soul in the other meaning is: an incorporeal substance that is a first perfection of a body moving it by choice from a rational principle, actual or potential. So that which is potential is the differentia of the human soul, and that which is actual is the differentia or property of the angelic soul. The explanation of the first definition: if a seed is thrown into the ground, and it becomes prepared for growth and nutrition, it changes from its former state before being placed in the ground. That is due to the occurrence of a quality in it – had it not been present, it would not have been prepared to receive it from the Bestower of forms, which is God Most High and His angels. That quality is a perfection for it, therefore it is said in the definition that it is a first perfection of the body, and this is put in the place of the genus. This is shared by the seed and the sperm of animal and man. So the soul is a form in relation to the mixed matter, since it is impressed in matter, and it is a power in relation to its act, and a perfection in relation to the plant and animal species. The signification of 'perfection' is more complete than that of 'power' and 'form'; therefore it is used in place of the genus. 'Natural' excludes artificial, for the forms of artifacts are also perfections in them. 'Organic' excludes the powers in the four elements, for they act not by organs but by themselves, while the psychic powers act by organs in them. Their statement 'having life potentially' is another differentia, meaning that it is such that it lives by growth and remains by nutrition, and perhaps it lives by sensation and motion which are in its potentiality. Their statement 'first perfection' with 'first' excludes the power of motion and sensation, for that is also a perfection of the body but it is not a first perfection; it occurs secondarily to the existence of the perfection which is the soul.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage defines the soul (nafs) as understood by the Peripatetic philosophers. The author begins by noting that 'soul' is a homonym (ism mushtarak) with two meanings. The first meaning is shared by humans, animals, and plants: a first perfection (kamal awwal) of a natural organic body (jism tabi'i ali) that has life potentially (dhu hayah bi-l-quwwa). This definition applies to vegetative and animal souls. The second meaning is shared by humans and celestial angels: an incorporeal substance (jawhar ghayr jism) that is a first perfection of a body, moving it by choice from a rational principle ('an mabda' nutqi 'aqli); this principle can be actual or potential. The potential differentia belongs to the human soul, the actual to the angelic soul. The author then explains the first definition in detail using the example of a seed thrown into the ground. The seed acquires a quality (sifa) that enables growth and nutrition; this quality is a perfection (kamal) from the Bestower of forms (wahib al-suwar), i.e., God and His angels. This perfection is called 'first perfection' (kamal awwal) of the body. The author clarifies that 'soul' is called a form (sura) in relation to matter, a power (quwwa) in relation to its act, and a perfection (kamal) in relation to the species. 'Perfection' is used as the genus because it is more comprehensive. 'Natural' (tabi'i) excludes artificial forms; 'organic' (ali) excludes the powers of the four elements which act without organs. 'Having life potentially' is the differentia, specifying that the soul is the principle of life functions. 'First perfection' excludes subsequent perfections like actual sensation and motion, which come after the soul is already present.

The Soul as a Homonym: Two Definitions
Presents nafs as a homonym with two definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of nafs and identify the differentia that separates the human soul from the angelic soul.

Core Idea
Nafs is a homonym: a first perfection for living bodies, and an incorporeal substance for rational souls.

The passage begins by stating that 'soul' is a homonym with two meanings: one shared by humans, animals, and plants; another shared by humans and angels. This lesson unpacks both definitions and highlights the key difference: the human soul's rational principle is potential, while the angelic is actual.

Guided Path
1
Find the two definitions
Locate the two definitions in the Arabic: the first is 'كمال جسم طبيعي آلي ذي حياة بالقوة' (first perfection of a natural organic body with potential life), the second is 'جوهر غير جسم هو كمال أول للجسم محرك له بالاختيار عن مبدأ نطقي' (incorporeal substance that is a first perfection of a body moving it by choice from a rational principle).
Notice that the second definition includes 'incorporeal substance' as the genus.
2
Compare the genera
The first definition has genus = 'first perfection', the second has genus = 'incorporeal substance'. This reflects that the second meaning applies to souls that are separate from matter (angels) and capable of rational choice, while the first applies to souls that are forms of material bodies.
The second definition is more specific to rational souls.
3
Analyze the differentia for the second definition
The differentia includes 'moves the body by choice from a rational principle'. The text further distinguishes between potential and actual: the human soul has this principle potentially, the angelic soul actually. This is the key difference between the two types of rational souls.
The text says: 'فالذي بالقوة هو فصل النفس الإنسانية، والذي بالفعل هو فصل أو خاصة للنفس الملكية'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفس فهو عندهم اسم مشترك يقع على معنى يشترك فيه الإنسان والحيوان والنبات وعلى معنى آخر يشترك فيه الإنسان والملائكة السماوية عندهم، فحدّ النفس بالمعنى الأول عندهم أنه كمال جسم طبيعي آلي ذي حياة بالقوة، وحدّ النفس بالمعنى الآخر أنه جوهر غير جسم هو كمال أول للجسم محرك له بالاختيار عن مبدأ نطقي أي عقلي بالفعل أو بالقوة، فالذي بالقوة هو فصل النفس الإنسانية، والذي بالفعل هو فصل أو خاصة للنفس الملكية. وشرح الحد الأول أن حبّة البذر إذا طُرحت في الأرض، فاستعدت للنمو والاغتذاء فقد تغيّرت عما كان عليه قبل طرحه في الأرض، وذلك بحدوث صفة فيه لو لم تكن . لما استعد لقبولها من واهب الصور، وهو الله تعالى وملائكته، فتلك الصفة كمال له فلذلك قيل في الحدّ إنه كمال أول للجسم، ووضع ذلك موضع الجنس، وهذا يشترك فيه البذر والنطفة للحيوان والإنسان. فالنفس صورة بالقياس إلى المادة الممتزجة، إذ هي منطبعة في المادة وهي قوة بالقياس إلى فعلها. وكمال بالقياس إلى النوع النباتي والحيواني ودلالة الكمال أتم من دلالة القوة والصورة، فلذلك عبر به في محلّ الجنس والطبيعي احتراز عن الصناعي فإن صور الصناعات أيضاً كمال فيها، والآلي احتراز عن القوى التي في العناصر الأربعة، فإنها تفعل لا بآلات بل بذواتها، والقوى النفسانية فعلها بآلات فيها وقولهم «ذو حياة بالقوة» فصل آخر أي من شأنه أن يحيا بالنشوء ويبقى بالغذاء، وربما يحيا بإحساس وحركة هما في قوته. وقولهم: «كمال أول الاحتراز بالأول عـن قـوة الـتـحـريـك والإحساس، فإنه أيضاً كمال للجسم لكنه ليس كمالاً أولاً يقع ثانياً لوجود الكمال الذي هو نفس.
As for the soul, it is in their view a homonym that applies to a meaning shared by man, animal, and plant, and to another meaning shared by man and the celestial angels in their view. The definition of the soul in the first meaning in their view is: a first perfection of a natural organic body having life potentially. The definition of the soul in the other meaning is: an incorporeal substance that is a first perfection of a body moving it by choice from a rational principle, actual or potential. So that which is potential is the differentia of the human soul, and that which is actual is the differentia or property of the angelic soul. The explanation of the first definition: if a seed is thrown into the ground, and it becomes prepared for growth and nutrition, it changes from its former state before being placed in the ground. That is due to the occurrence of a quality in it – had it not been present, it would not have been prepared to receive it from the Bestower of forms, which is God Most High and His angels. That quality is a perfection for it, therefore it is said in the definition that it is a first perfection of the body, and this is put in the place of the genus. This is shared by the seed and the sperm of animal and man. So the soul is a form in relation to the mixed matter, since it is impressed in matter, and it is a power in relation to its act, and a perfection in relation to the plant and animal species. The signification of 'perfection' is more complete than that of 'power' and 'form'; therefore it is used in place of the genus. 'Natural' excludes artificial, for the forms of artifacts are also perfections in them. 'Organic' excludes the powers in the four elements, for they act not by organs but by themselves, while the psychic powers act by organs in them. Their statement 'having life potentially' is another differentia, meaning that it is such that it lives by growth and remains by nutrition, and perhaps it lives by sensation and motion which are in its potentiality. Their statement 'first perfection' with 'first' excludes the power of motion and sensation, for that is also a perfection of the body but it is not a first perfection; it occurs secondarily to the existence of the perfection which is the soul.
Source pages p. 149

The second meaning is shared by humans and celestial angels: an incorporeal substance (jawhar ghayr jism) that is a first perfection of a body, moving it by choice from a rational principle ('an mabda' nutqi 'aqli); this principle can be actual or potential. The potential differentia belongs to the human soul, the actual to the angelic soul. The author then explains the first definition in detail using the example of a seed thrown into the ground. The seed acquires a quality (sifa) that enables growth and nutrition; this quality is a perfection (kamal) from the Bestower of forms (wahib al-suwar), i.e., God and His angels. Universal Intellect and Intellect of the Whole: Key Distinctions
Defines universal intellect and explains two meanings of intellect of the whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the universal intellect and the two meanings of the intellect of the whole, and locate them within the Peripatetic classification of existents.

Core Idea
The universal intellect is a mental universal; the intellect of the whole is a cosmic hierarchy.

This passage from the Mi'yar al-'Ilm introduces four terms related to intellect and soul. The author first classifies existents into three types, then defines the universal intellect as an abstract concept, and finally gives two meanings for the intellect of the whole. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for navigating Peripatetic epistemology and cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the threefold classification
Find the sentence 'الموجودات عندهم ثلاثة أقسام' and note the three types: bodies, active intellects, souls.
The active intellects are free from matter; souls are intermediaries.
2
Locate the definition of universal intellect
Find the definition starting 'والعقل الكلّي يعنون به المعنى المعقول...' and note that it is predicated on many but exists only in conception.
3
Analyze the example of 'universal man'
Understand that the form of man abstracted from Zayd corresponds to all individual humans but is not identical to any.
This illustrates that universals have no concrete existence.
4
Find the two meanings of intellect of the whole
The text says 'يطلق على معنيين'. Identify the first meaning (totality of immaterial essences) and the second (celestial body).
5
Distinguish the two terms
Compare 'aql kulli (mental universal) with 'aql al-kull (either hierarchy of separate intellects or celestial body).
Do not confuse the universal concept with a separate substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقل الكلّي وعقل الكلّ والنفس الكلّي ونفس الكل، فبيانه أن الموجودات عندهم ثلاثة أقسام: أجسام وهي أختها، وعقول فعالة أشرفها لبراءتها عن المادة وعلاقة المادة، أنها لا تحرك المواد أيضاً إلا بالشوق حتى وأوسطها النفوس وهي التي تنفعل من العقل وتفعل في الأجسام، وهي واسطة، ويعنون بالملائكة السماوية نفوس الأفلاك فإنها حيّة عندهم وبالملائكة المُقرَّبين العقول الفعّالة. والعقل الكلّي يعنون به المعنى المعقول المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالعدد من العقول التي لأشخاص الناس، ولا وجود لها في القوام بل في التصوّر، فإنك إذا قلت : الإنسان الكلّي أشرت به إلى المعنى المعقول من الإنسان الموجود في سائر الأشخاص، الذي هو للعقل صورة واحدة تطابق سائر أشخاص الناس، ولا وجود لإنسانية واحدة هي إنسانية زيد وهي بعينها إنسانية عمرو، ولكن في العقل تحصل صورة الإنسان من شخص زيد مثلاً، ويطابق سائر أشخاص الناس كلّهم فَيُسَمّى ذلك الإنسانية الكلية، فهذا ما يعنون بالعقل الكلّي.
وأما عقل الكلّ فيطلق على معنيين: أحدهما وهو الأوفق للفظ إن يُراد بالكلّ جملة العالم، فعقل الكلّ على هذا المعنى بمعنى شرح اسمه أنه جملة الذوات المجردة عن المادة ، من جميع الجهات التي لا تتحرّك لا بالذات ولا بالعَرَض . ولا تحرك إلا بالشوق وآخر رتبة هذه الجملة هي العقل الفعال المخرج للنفس الإنسانية في العلوم العقلية من القوة إلى الفعل، وهذه الجملة هي مبادي الكلّ بعد المبدأ الأول. والمبدأ الأول هو مبدع الكلّ، وأما الكلّ بالمعنى الثاني فهو الجرم
As for the universal intellect, the intellect of the whole, the universal soul, and the soul of the whole, their explanation is that existents in their view are three divisions: bodies and their kin, active intellects—the most noble due to their freedom from matter and material attachment—yet they also do not move matter except by longing—and the middle are souls, which are acted upon by intellect and act upon bodies, and they are intermediaries. By the celestial angels they mean the souls of the spheres, for they are living in their view, and by the proximate angels they mean the active intellects. They mean by the universal intellect the intelligible meaning predicated on many numerically different intellects of individual humans, which has no existence in concrete reality but only in conceptualization. For if you say 'the universal man', you refer to the intelligible meaning of man existing in all individuals, which is for the intellect a single form corresponding to all individual humans. There is no single humanity that is the humanity of Zayd and is the very same humanity of Amr, but in the intellect a form of man is obtained from the person of Zayd, for example, and it corresponds to all individual humans, so that is called universal humanity. This is what they mean by the universal intellect. As for the intellect of the whole, it is applied to two meanings. One of them—and it is more concordant with the wording—is that by 'the whole' is meant the entirety of the world. So the intellect of the whole in this sense means, in explanation of its name, the totality of essences abstracted from matter from all aspects, which do not move either essentially or accidentally, and only move by longing. The lowest rank of this totality is the active intellect that brings out the human soul in intellectual sciences from potentiality to actuality. This totality is the principles of the whole after the First Principle. The First Principle is the Creator of the whole. As for 'the whole' in the second sense, it is the celestial body.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage explains four related terms: universal intellect ('aql kulli), intellect of the whole ('aql al-kull), universal soul (nafs kulli), and soul of the whole (nafs al-kull). The author first situates these terms within the Peripatetic classification of existents into three categories: bodies (ajsam), active intellects ('uqul fa''ala), and souls (nufus). The active intellects are the noblest due to their freedom from matter; they move material things only by longing (shawq). Souls are intermediates, acted upon by intellect and acting on bodies. The celestial angels are identified with the souls of the spheres, and the proximate angels (al-mala'ika al-muqarrabun) are the active intellects. The author then defines 'universal intellect' as the intelligible meaning (ma'na ma'qul) that is predicated on many numerically distinct individual human intellects. It exists only in conception (tasawwur), not in concrete reality (la wujud laha fi al-qiwam). An example is the concept of 'universal man' (al-insan al-kulli): when you say 'man', you refer to a form that corresponds to all individual humans, but it is not a single humanity existing in Zayd and Amr; it is a mental form abstracted from individuals. Finally, 'intellect of the whole' has two meanings. The first, more literal, meaning: taking 'the whole' (al-kull) as the entire universe, the intellect of the whole is the totality of immaterial substances (al-dhawat al-mujarrada 'an al-madda) that neither move essentially nor accidentally, and only move by longing. The lowest in this hierarchy is the active intellect. This totality constitutes the principles (mabadi') of the whole after the First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal), who is the Creator (mubdi') of the whole. The second meaning of 'the whole' is the celestial body (al-jirm).

Key Terms
العقل الكلّي
عقل الكلّ
النفس الكلّي
الملائكة المقربون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A separate substance that moves the spheres.
B) The totality of active intellects.
C) The intelligible meaning that corresponds to many individual intellects, existing only in conception.
D) The first principle of the universe.

Key Takeaway
The universal intellect is a mental universal, whereas the intellect of the whole is a collection of separate substances or a celestial body.

Defining the Universal Soul as a Universal Term
Author defines the universal soul as a universal term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the genus and differentia from the definition of a universal term (kulli) as applied to the universal soul.

Core Idea
A universal is an intelligible meaning shared by many individuals — the soul is no exception.

In this passage, al-Ghazali defines the universal soul by its logical property: it is an intelligible meaning predicated of many individual souls. This mirrors the standard definition of a universal term in Aristotelian logic. Let's dissect the definition to see its components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term defined
The term being defined is 'al-nafs al-kulli' (the universal soul). Find where it is introduced in the Arabic.
2
Locate the definition formula
The definition reads: 'al-ma'na al-ma'qul al-maqul 'ala kathirin mukhtalifin fi al-'adad' (the intelligible meaning predicated of many differing in number).
3
Separate genus and differentia
The genus is 'intelligible meaning' (ma'na ma'qul). The differentia is 'predicated of many differing in number' (maqul 'ala kathirin mukhtalifin fi al-'adad).
This is the standard logical definition of any universal (kulli), not a unique definition of soul.
4
Compare with universal intellect
The passage says 'as we mentioned in the universal intellect.' Recall that the universal intellect was defined similarly as an intelligible meaning predicated of many.
Both are universals; their differentia may relate to their specific content.
5
Assess definition type
Since it answers 'what is it' (fi jawab ma hiya), this is a real definition (hadd haqiqi) of the term 'universal soul' as a universal. But note it does not specify the essence of 'soul' as a substance—that comes later in the passage.
The metaphysical definition (totality of non-corporeal substances) is a different description (rasm).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفس الكلي فالمراد به المعنى المعقول المقول على كثيرين مختلفين في العدد، في جواب ما هو التي كلّ واحدة منها نفس خاصة لشخص، كما ذكرنا في العقل الكلّي. ونفس الكلّ على قياس عقل الكلّ جملة الجواهر الغير الجسمانية، التي كمالات مدبرة للأجسام السماوية المحركة لها على سبيل الاختيار العقلي ونسبة نفس الكلّ إلى عقل الكلّ كنسبة أنفسنا إلى العقل الفعّال. ونفس الكلّ هو مبدأ قريب لوجود الأجسام الطبيعية ومرتبة في نيل الوجود بعد مرتبة عقل الكلّ، ووجوده فائض عن وجوده.
As for the universal soul, what is meant by it is the intelligible meaning predicated of many differing in number, in answer to 'what is it' which each one of them is a particular soul for a person, as we mentioned in the universal intellect. And the universal soul, analogously to the universal intellect, is the totality of non-corporeal substances, which are perfections that manage the celestial bodies, moving them by way of intellectual choice. And the relation of the universal soul to the universal intellect is like the relation of our souls to the active intellect. And the universal soul is a proximate principle for the existence of natural bodies and a rank in attaining existence after the rank of the universal intellect, and its existence flows from its existence.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage defines the universal soul (al-nafs al-kullī) in philosophical terms. It begins by stating that the universal soul is an intelligible concept predicated of many individual souls, each of which is a particular soul for a person, similar to the universal intellect defined earlier. Then it explains that the universal soul is the sum of non-corporeal substances, which are perfections that manage the celestial bodies, moving them by intellectual choice, not by force. The relation of the universal soul to the universal intellect is compared to the relation of our souls to the active intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), a standard Neoplatonic concept. Finally, it states that the universal soul is the proximate principle of natural bodies, coming after the universal intellect in the hierarchy of existence, and its existence emanates from the universal intellect. All examples here are from philosophical cosmology, not from revealed sciences, but al-Ghazali is expounding the philosophers' view.

Key Terms
النفس الكلي
العقل الفعّال
عقل الكلّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A non-corporeal substance that manages celestial bodies.
B) An intelligible meaning predicated of many differing in number.
C) The active intellect that emanates human souls.
D) The first emanation from God, identified with the Throne.

Key Takeaway
The universal soul is a universal term, defined logically by its predictability of many individual souls.

The Cosmic Hierarchy: Universal Soul's Position
Author describes the metaphysical hierarchy and analogy involving the universal soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to map the hierarchical relations among the universal soul, universal intellect, and active intellect, and explain the analogy used in the passage.

Core Idea
The universal soul is to the universal intellect as our souls are to the active intellect — an analogy linking macrocosm to microcosm.

Al-Ghazali not only defines the universal soul logically but also places it in a cosmic hierarchy. He makes an explicit analogy that connects the celestial realm to human experience. Let's unpack this analogy and the relational structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three entities
List: universal soul (nafs kulli), universal intellect ('aql kull), active intellect ('aql fa''al). Note that 'our souls' are also mentioned.
2
Locate the analogy
The passage says: 'Wa nisbat nafs al-kull ila 'aql al-kull kanisbat anfusina ila al-'aql al-fa''al.' (And the relation of the universal soul to the universal intellect is like the relation of our souls to the active intellect.)
3
Interpret each side of the analogy
Just as our souls derive from and depend on the active intellect for their existence and actualization, the universal soul derives from and depends on the universal intellect.
4
Determine the order of emanation
The passage states that the universal soul is 'a rank in attaining existence after the rank of the universal intellect, and its existence flows from its existence.' Thus: universal intellect -> universal soul -> natural bodies.
5
Connect to Islamic cosmology (optional)
In some interpretations, the universal intellect is identified with the Throne ('Arsh) and the universal soul with the Pedestal (Kursi). Though not explicit here, this links the passage to broader Islamic thought.
This connection is not required for understanding the passage but provides context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النفس الكلي فالمراد به المعنى المعقول المقول على كثيرين مختلفين في العدد، في جواب ما هو التي كلّ واحدة منها نفس خاصة لشخص، كما ذكرنا في العقل الكلّي. ونفس الكلّ على قياس عقل الكلّ جملة الجواهر الغير الجسمانية، التي كمالات مدبرة للأجسام السماوية المحركة لها على سبيل الاختيار العقلي ونسبة نفس الكلّ إلى عقل الكلّ كنسبة أنفسنا إلى العقل الفعّال. ونفس الكلّ هو مبدأ قريب لوجود الأجسام الطبيعية ومرتبة في نيل الوجود بعد مرتبة عقل الكلّ، ووجوده فائض عن وجوده.
As for the universal soul, what is meant by it is the intelligible meaning predicated of many differing in number, in answer to 'what is it' which each one of them is a particular soul for a person, as we mentioned in the universal intellect. And the universal soul, analogously to the universal intellect, is the totality of non-corporeal substances, which are perfections that manage the celestial bodies, moving them by way of intellectual choice. And the relation of the universal soul to the universal intellect is like the relation of our souls to the active intellect. And the universal soul is a proximate principle for the existence of natural bodies and a rank in attaining existence after the rank of the universal intellect, and its existence flows from its existence.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage defines the universal soul (al-nafs al-kullī) in philosophical terms. It begins by stating that the universal soul is an intelligible concept predicated of many individual souls, each of which is a particular soul for a person, similar to the universal intellect defined earlier. Then it explains that the universal soul is the sum of non-corporeal substances, which are perfections that manage the celestial bodies, moving them by intellectual choice, not by force. The relation of the universal soul to the universal intellect is compared to the relation of our souls to the active intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), a standard Neoplatonic concept. Finally, it states that the universal soul is the proximate principle of natural bodies, coming after the universal intellect in the hierarchy of existence, and its existence emanates from the universal intellect. All examples here are from philosophical cosmology, not from revealed sciences, but al-Ghazali is expounding the philosophers' view.

عقل الكلّ

Analyzing the Philosophers' Definition of Angel
Author defines the angel as a simple substance with life and rational speech, intermediary between God and earthly bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the philosophers' definition of angel and compare it with the kalam definition.

Core Idea
The philosophers define angel as a simple substance—a genus that kalam rejects.

This passage presents the philosophers' definition of an angel. We will break it down into its logical components: genus and differentia. Then we will compare this with the standard Islamic theological (kalam) definition to understand the philosophical shift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The definiendum is 'al-malak' (the angel). Note that the definition is introduced with 'wa ḥadd al-malak' (and the definition of the angel). The phrase 'this is its definition according to them' signals that this is the philosophers' position, not necessarily the author's.
2
Extract the genus
The genus given is 'jawhar basīṭ' (simple substance). This means an entity with no parts. In Aristotelian ontology, 'substance' is a category; 'simple' distinguishes it from composite bodies. Write down the genus as 'simple substance'.
3
Identify the differentiae
The definition lists three differentiae: (1) possessing life (dhū ḥayāh), (2) possessing rational speech (nāṭiq ʿaqlī), and (3) being immortal (ghayr māʾit). Together these distinguish angels from other simple substances like intellects or souls. Note also the classification into intellectual and psychic angels.
4
Contrast with the kalam definition
In kalam, angels are defined as 'subtle bodies' (ajsām laṭīfa) capable of assuming forms. They are not simple substances but have some corporeality. Compare: the philosophers' genus is 'simple substance'; the mutakallimūn's genus is 'body'. This shift changes theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحدّ الملك أنه جوهر بسيط ذو حياة ونطق عقلي غير مائت، هو واسطة بين الباري عزّ وجلّ والأجسام الأرضية فمنه عقلي ومنه نفسي؛ هذا حده عندهم.
And the definition of the angel is that it is a simple substance possessing life and rational speech, not mortal; it is an intermediary between the Exalted Creator and the earthly bodies; some of it are intellectual and some of it are psychic; this is its definition according to them.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage provides the philosophers' definition of an angel. It states that an angel is a simple substance (jawhar basīṭ), meaning it has no parts. It possesses life (ḥayāh) and rational speech (nāṭiq ʿaqlī), and is not mortal (ghayr mā'it). The angel serves as an intermediary (wāsiṭah) between God (al-Bārī) and the earthly bodies. Angels are divided into two types: intellectual (ʿaqlī) and psychic (nafsī), meaning some are pure intellects and some are souls. The phrase 'according to them' indicates this is the philosophers' definition, not necessarily al-Ghazali's own view, as he often critiques Aristotelian philosophy.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define angels as simple substances with life and rational speech, a definition that contrasts with the kalam view of angels as subtle bodies.

The Philosophers' Definitions of Cause and Effect and the Min/Maʿa Distinction
Author defines cause and effect, distinguishing between 'from its existence' and 'with its existence'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to (1) state the philosophers' definitions of cause and effect, (2) describe the three modal states of the effect, and (3) distinguish causal dependence from mere concomitance using the key phrases 'min wujūdihi' and 'maʿa wujūdihi'.

Core Idea
Cause necessitates; effect depends; the effect is possible in itself but necessary by the cause; 'from its existence' is not 'with its existence'.

This passage from al-Ghazali's Miʿyar al-ʿIlm presents the precise definitions of cause and effect according to the philosophers (falāsifa). It also draws a critical distinction between 'from its existence' (min wujūdihi), which indicates genuine causal dependence, and 'with its existence' (maʿa wujūdihi), which indicates mere mutual entailment. Mastering this distinction is essential for understanding later kalam critiques of causality and for analyzing logical arguments about divine action and cosmic contingency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definienda
Locate the terms 'al-ʿilla' (cause) and 'al-maʿlūl' (effect) in the Arabic text. Read their definitions carefully.
The cause is defined as an essence from which the existence of another actualizes, not vice versa. The effect is defined as an essence whose existence is actual from another.
2
Extract the three modal states of the effect
The passage states that the effect, considered in itself, has pure possibility (al-imkān al-maḥḍ). With the cause, it becomes necessary; without the cause, it becomes impossible.
These three states are crucial for arguments about contingency: the effect is contingent in itself but necessitated by the cause.
3
Find the two key phrases 'min wujūdihi' and 'maʿa wujūdihi'
Search for these phrases in the Arabic. The passage explicitly says that the meaning of 'min wujūdihi' is different from the meaning of 'maʿa wujūdihi'.
This is the central conceptual distinction in the passage.
4
Define each phrase based on the passage
'Min wujūdihi' means that the effect's existence is actualized from the cause, implying priority and dependence. 'Maʿa wujūdihi' means mutual entailment: if one is assumed, the other follows; if one is removed, the other is removed.
Note that 'maʿa wujūdihi' is symmetric, while 'min wujūdihi' is asymmetric.
5
Analyze the asymmetry of entailments
The passage explains that assuming the effect exists entails that the cause existed before. Assuming the cause exists entails the effect follows. Removing the cause necessitates removing the effect, but removing the effect does not necessitate removing the cause; rather it indicates the cause was already removed.
This asymmetry is the core of causal priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحدّ العلة عندهم أنها كلّ ذات وجود ذات آخر إنما هو بالفعل من وجود هذا الفعل ووجود هذا بالفعل ليس من وجود ذلك بالفعل. وأما المعلول فهو كلّ ذات وجوده بالفعل من وجود غيره، ووجود ذلك الغير ليس من وجوده. ومعنى قولنا من وجوده غير معنى قولنا مع وجوده، فإن معنى أن قولنا: من وجوده هو تكون الذات باعتبار نفسها ممكنة الوجود، وإنما يجب وجودها بالفعل لا من ذاتها، بل لأن ذاتاً أخرى موجودة بالفعل يلزم عنها وجوب هذه الذات، ويكون لها في نفسها الإمكان المحض، ولها في نفسها بشرط العلّة الوجوب، ولها في نفسها بشرط عدم العلّة الامتناع. وأما قولنا: مع وجوده فهو أن يكون كلّ واحدٍ من الذاتين فرض موجوداً لزم أن يعلم أن الآخر موجود، وإذا فرض مرفوعاً لزم أن الآخر مرفوع، والعلة والمعلول معاً بمعنى هذين اللزومين، وإن كان بين وجهي اللزومين اختلاف لأن أحدهما وهو المعلول، إذا فرض موجوداً لزم أن يكون الآخر قد كان موجوداً حتى وجد هذا. وأما الآخر، وهو العلة، فإذا فرض موجوداً لزم أن يتبع وجوده وجود المعلول، وإذا كان المعلول مرفوعاً لزم أن يحكم أن العلة كانت أولاً مرفوعة حتى رفع هذا، لا أن رفع المعلول أوجب رفع العلّة، وأما العلة فإذا رفعناها وجب رفع المعلول بإيجاب رفع العلة.
And the definition of the cause according to them is that it is every essence whose existence of another is actual only from the existence of this act, and the existence of this actually is not from the existence of that actually. As for the effect, it is every essence whose existence is actual from the existence of another, and the existence of that other is not from its existence. And the meaning of our saying 'from its existence' is other than the meaning of our saying 'with its existence'. For the meaning of our saying 'from its existence' is that the essence, considered in itself, is possible of existence, and its existence becomes actual not from itself, but because another essence already actual necessitates the necessity of this essence, and it has in itself pure possibility, and it has in itself with the condition of the cause necessity, and it has in itself with the condition of the absence of the cause impossibility. As for our saying 'with its existence', it is that each of the two essences, if assumed existing, entails that the other is known to exist, and if assumed removed, entails that the other is removed. And the cause and effect together are in the sense of these two entailments, although there is a difference between the two aspects of entailment, because one of them, namely the effect, if assumed existing, entails that the other must have already existed so that this existed. As for the other, namely the cause, if assumed existing, entails that the existence of the effect follows its existence, and if the effect is removed, it entails that the cause was first removed so that this is removed, not that the removal of the effect caused the removal of the cause. And as for the cause, if we remove it, the removal of the effect becomes necessary by the necessitation of the removal of the cause.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage defines the cause (al-ʿilla) and effect (al-maʿlūl) according to the philosophers, with careful distinction between two relational meanings. It begins with the definition: a cause is an essence from which the existence of another essence actualizes, while the effect is an essence whose existence actualizes from another. The key conceptual distinction is between 'from its existence' (min wujūdihi) and 'with its existence' (maʿa wujūdihi). 'From its existence' implies that the essence itself is possible in itself, but becomes necessary through another essence. The effect has pure possibility in itself, necessity with the cause, and impossibility without the cause. 'With its existence' simply means that if one is assumed to exist, the other follows; and if one is assumed removed, the other is removed. However, there is an asymmetry: the effect's existence presupposes the prior existence of the cause, whereas the cause's existence necessitates the effect's existence. The removal of the cause necessitates the removal of the effect, but not vice versa. This is a standard philosophical analysis of causation, emphasizing the asymmetry between cause and effect. Al-Ghazali presents this as the philosophers' view, which he later critiques.

Key Terms
العلة
المعلول
الإمكان المحض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Necessary existence.
B) Impossible existence.
C) Pure possibility.
D) Actual existence.

Key Takeaway
Cause and effect are asymmetrically related: the effect is possible in itself, necessary by the cause, and impossible without it; 'from its existence' denotes causal dependence, while 'with its existence' denotes mere concomitance.

?

According to the philosophers' definition, is the world (as effect) purely possible in itself? How does the concept of 'min wujūdihi' apply to this argument? Use the three modal states to explain.

Definition of Ibda': Creation without Intermediary
Author defines ibda' as a homonym with two concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of ibda' and recognize the First Intellect as an example of the second meaning.

Core Idea
Ibda' means creation without intermediary, either absolute or from a cause without medium.

In Islamic metaphysics, the term ibda' (إبداع) is crucial for describing how the First Intellect originates from God. The author defines it as a homonym with two meanings, both involving creation without an intermediate cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term ibda' in the Arabic. It is defined as a homonym (ism mushtarak) with two concepts.
The passage begins with 'حد الإبداع'.
2
Extract the first meaning
The first meaning is: 'تأسيس الشيء لا عن ولا بواسطة شيء' – establishment of a thing not from nor through any intermediary. This is creation ex nihilo.
3
Extract the second meaning
The second meaning is: 'أن يكون للشيء وجود مطلق عن سبب بلا متوسط' – absolute existence from a cause without an intermediary, with the thing being possible in itself.
The thing's existence is not self-grounded.
4
Apply the example
The First Intellect (al-'aql al-awwal) is given as an example of the second meaning. It is a mubdi' because its existence is from God, not from itself.
The text says: 'وبهذا المفهوم العقل الأول مبدع'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مادة
حد الإبداع : هو اسم مشترك لمفهومين: أحدهما تأسيس الشيء لا عن ولا بواسطة شيء، والمفهوم الثاني أن يكون للشيء وجود مطلق عن سبب بلا متوسط، وله في ذاته أن لا يكون موجوداً، وقد أفقد الذي له في ذاته إفقاداً تاماً. وبهذا المفهوم العقل الأول مبدع في كلّ حال لأنه ليس وجوده من ذاته، فله من ذاته العدم، وقد أفقد ذلك إفقاداً تاماً.
حد الخلق : هو اسم مشترك، فقد يقال خلق لإفادة وجود كيف كان. وقد يقال : خلق لإفادة وجود حاصل عن مادة وصورة كيف كان وقد يقال : خلق لهذا المعنى الثاني لكن بطريق الاختراع من غير سبق مادة فيها قوة وجوده وإمكانه .
حد الإحداث : هو اسم مشترك يُطلق على وجهين ؛ أحدهما زماني، ومعنى الإحداث الزماني الإيجاد للشيء بعد أن لم يكن له وجود في زمان سابق، ومعنى الإحداث غير الزماني هو إفادة الشيء وجوداً، وذلك الشيء ليس له في ذاته ذلك الوجود، لا بحسب زمان دون زمان بل بحسب كل زمان .
حد القدم والقدم يقال على وجوه ، يقال : قدم بالقياس، وقدم مطلق، والقدم بالقياس هو شيء زمانه في الماضي أكثر من زمان شيء آخر، فهو قدم بالقياس إليه . وأما القدم المطلق فهو أيضاً على وجهين : يقال بحسب الزمان، وبحسب الذات، فأما الذي بحسب الزمان فهو الشيء الذي وُجد في زمان ماض غير متناه. وأما القديم بحسب الذات فهو الذي ليس لوجود ذاته مبدأ به وجب، فالقديم بحسب الزمان هو الذي ليس له وجود زماني وهو موجود والسموات وجملة أصول العالم عندهم، والقديم بحسب الذات هو الذي ليس له مبدأ علي أي ليس له علة وليس ذلك إلا الباري عزّ وجلّ.
Section. The definition of ibda': it is a homonym for two concepts: one is the establishment of a thing not from nor through any intermediary, and the second concept is that a thing has absolute existence from a cause without an intermediary, and it is in itself possible to be non-existent, and that which has in itself [non-existence] is entirely deprived. By this concept, the First Intellect is a mubdi' in every state because its existence is not from itself, so from itself it has non-existence, and that has been entirely removed. The definition of khalq: it is a homonym. Sometimes khalq is said to mean bringing existence however it is. Sometimes khalq is said to mean bringing existence resulting from matter and form however it is. And sometimes khalq is said for this second meaning but by way of invention without prior matter having the potentiality of its existence and possibility. The definition of ihdath: it is a homonym applied in two ways: one temporal, the meaning of temporal ihdath is bringing a thing into existence after it had no existence in a prior time; the meaning of non-temporal ihdath is giving a thing existence, which thing does not have that existence in itself, not according to one time but according to every time. The definition of qidam: qidam is said in several ways. It is said: relative qidam, and absolute qidam. Relative qidam is something whose past time is greater than the past time of another thing, so it is qidam relative to it. As for absolute qidam, it is also of two types: according to time and according to essence. As for that according to time, it is the thing that existed in an infinite past time. As for the eternal according to essence, it is that which has no beginning for its existence by which it is necessitated. So the eternal according to time is that which has no temporal existence, and it exists, and the heavens and all the principles of the world are such according to them. The eternal according to essence is that which has no beginning, i.e., no cause, and that is nothing but the Creator, exalted and majestic.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents definitions of four critical terms in Islamic cosmology and metaphysics: ibda' (creation ex nihilo), khalq (creation in general), ihdath (origination), and qidam (eternity). The author states that each term is a homonym (ism mushtarak) with multiple meanings. 

For ibda', two meanings are given: (1) establishing a thing from nothing and without any intermediary, and (2) absolute existence from a cause without an intermediary, where the thing is possible in itself. The First Intellect (al-'aql al-awwal) is an example of an ibda' in the second sense. 

For khalq, three meanings: (1) bringing existence in any manner, (2) bringing existence from matter and form, and (3) invention (ikhtira') without prior matter having the potential for that existence. 

For ihdath, two meanings: temporal (zamanī) meaning bringing into existence after previous non-existence in time, and non-temporal meaning giving existence to something that does not possess that existence in itself, at all times. 

For qidam, the author distinguishes relative qidam (having a longer past than another), absolute qidam by time (existing in infinite past time, like the heavens according to philosophers), and absolute qidam by essence (having no beginning and no cause, exclusive to Allah). These definitions are crucial for differentiating between the created and the eternal in kalam and falsafa.

Key Terms
الإبداع
العقل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the first meaning of ibda' involve?
A) Existence from a cause without intermediary
B) Establishment of a thing not from nor through any intermediary
C) Invention without prior matter
D) Relative eternity

Key Takeaway
Ibda' has two meanings: absolute creation from nothing, and existence from a cause without intermediary, exemplified by the First Intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the definition of ibda' and determine which meaning applies to the creation of the universe in the Ash'ari view (direct creation by God without intermediate causes).
?

Definition of Khalq: Three Modes of Creation
Author defines khalq as a homonym with three meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three meanings of khalq and distinguish innovation (ikhtira') from other types.

Core Idea
Khalq is a homonym for three modes of bringing existence.

The term khalq (خلق) is broader than ibda' and can refer to creation in various ways. The author distinguishes three meanings, including a specific meaning that corresponds to invention from no prior matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term khalq in the Arabic. It is introduced as a homonym (ism mushtarak).
The passage begins with 'حد الخلق'.
2
Extract the first meaning
The first meaning is 'إفادة وجود كيف كان' – bringing existence in any manner. This is the most general meaning.
3
Extract the second meaning
The second meaning is 'إفادة وجود حاصل عن مادة وصورة كيف كان' – bringing existence resulting from matter and form. This implies natural generation.
4
Extract the third meaning
The third meaning is the same as the second but 'بطريق الاختراع من غير سبق مادة فيها قوة وجوده' – by way of invention without prior matter having the potential for that existence.
This is often used for the creation of substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مادة
حد الإبداع : هو اسم مشترك لمفهومين: أحدهما تأسيس الشيء لا عن ولا بواسطة شيء، والمفهوم الثاني أن يكون للشيء وجود مطلق عن سبب بلا متوسط، وله في ذاته أن لا يكون موجوداً، وقد أفقد الذي له في ذاته إفقاداً تاماً. وبهذا المفهوم العقل الأول مبدع في كلّ حال لأنه ليس وجوده من ذاته، فله من ذاته العدم، وقد أفقد ذلك إفقاداً تاماً.
حد الخلق : هو اسم مشترك، فقد يقال خلق لإفادة وجود كيف كان. وقد يقال : خلق لإفادة وجود حاصل عن مادة وصورة كيف كان وقد يقال : خلق لهذا المعنى الثاني لكن بطريق الاختراع من غير سبق مادة فيها قوة وجوده وإمكانه .
حد الإحداث : هو اسم مشترك يُطلق على وجهين ؛ أحدهما زماني، ومعنى الإحداث الزماني الإيجاد للشيء بعد أن لم يكن له وجود في زمان سابق، ومعنى الإحداث غير الزماني هو إفادة الشيء وجوداً، وذلك الشيء ليس له في ذاته ذلك الوجود، لا بحسب زمان دون زمان بل بحسب كل زمان .
حد القدم والقدم يقال على وجوه ، يقال : قدم بالقياس، وقدم مطلق، والقدم بالقياس هو شيء زمانه في الماضي أكثر من زمان شيء آخر، فهو قدم بالقياس إليه . وأما القدم المطلق فهو أيضاً على وجهين : يقال بحسب الزمان، وبحسب الذات، فأما الذي بحسب الزمان فهو الشيء الذي وُجد في زمان ماض غير متناه. وأما القديم بحسب الذات فهو الذي ليس لوجود ذاته مبدأ به وجب، فالقديم بحسب الزمان هو الذي ليس له وجود زماني وهو موجود والسموات وجملة أصول العالم عندهم، والقديم بحسب الذات هو الذي ليس له مبدأ علي أي ليس له علة وليس ذلك إلا الباري عزّ وجلّ.
Section. The definition of ibda': it is a homonym for two concepts: one is the establishment of a thing not from nor through any intermediary, and the second concept is that a thing has absolute existence from a cause without an intermediary, and it is in itself possible to be non-existent, and that which has in itself [non-existence] is entirely deprived. By this concept, the First Intellect is a mubdi' in every state because its existence is not from itself, so from itself it has non-existence, and that has been entirely removed. The definition of khalq: it is a homonym. Sometimes khalq is said to mean bringing existence however it is. Sometimes khalq is said to mean bringing existence resulting from matter and form however it is. And sometimes khalq is said for this second meaning but by way of invention without prior matter having the potentiality of its existence and possibility. The definition of ihdath: it is a homonym applied in two ways: one temporal, the meaning of temporal ihdath is bringing a thing into existence after it had no existence in a prior time; the meaning of non-temporal ihdath is giving a thing existence, which thing does not have that existence in itself, not according to one time but according to every time. The definition of qidam: qidam is said in several ways. It is said: relative qidam, and absolute qidam. Relative qidam is something whose past time is greater than the past time of another thing, so it is qidam relative to it. As for absolute qidam, it is also of two types: according to time and according to essence. As for that according to time, it is the thing that existed in an infinite past time. As for the eternal according to essence, it is that which has no beginning for its existence by which it is necessitated. So the eternal according to time is that which has no temporal existence, and it exists, and the heavens and all the principles of the world are such according to them. The eternal according to essence is that which has no beginning, i.e., no cause, and that is nothing but the Creator, exalted and majestic.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents definitions of four critical terms in Islamic cosmology and metaphysics: ibda' (creation ex nihilo), khalq (creation in general), ihdath (origination), and qidam (eternity). The author states that each term is a homonym (ism mushtarak) with multiple meanings. 

For ibda', two meanings are given: (1) establishing a thing from nothing and without any intermediary, and (2) absolute existence from a cause without an intermediary, where the thing is possible in itself. The First Intellect (al-'aql al-awwal) is an example of an ibda' in the second sense. 

For khalq, three meanings: (1) bringing existence in any manner, (2) bringing existence from matter and form, and (3) invention (ikhtira') without prior matter having the potential for that existence. 

For ihdath, two meanings: temporal (zamanī) meaning bringing into existence after previous non-existence in time, and non-temporal meaning giving existence to something that does not possess that existence in itself, at all times. 

For qidam, the author distinguishes relative qidam (having a longer past than another), absolute qidam by time (existing in infinite past time, like the heavens according to philosophers), and absolute qidam by essence (having no beginning and no cause, exclusive to Allah). These definitions are crucial for differentiating between the created and the eternal in kalam and falsafa.

Key Terms
الخلق
الاختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which meaning of khalq involves prior matter and form?
A) First meaning
B) Second meaning
C) Third meaning
D) None

Key Takeaway
Khalq has three meanings: general existence, existence from matter and form, and invention from no prior matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following as one of the three meanings of khalq: a) the creation of humans from clay, b) the creation of angels from light, c) the general act of creating the universe.

Definition of Ihdath: Temporal and Non-Temporal Origination
Author defines ihdath as a homonym for temporal and non-temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish temporal from non-temporal ihdath and apply the distinction to the createdness of the world.

Core Idea
Ihdath contrasts temporal origination with the giving of existence at all times.

Ihdath (إحداث) is a key term in the debate on the world's temporal creation. The author differentiates between temporal origination (after a period of non-existence) and non-temporal origination (giving existence to something that is inherently nonexistent).

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term ihdath in the Arabic. It is a homonym applied in two ways.
The passage begins with 'حد الإحداث'.
2
Extract the temporal meaning
Temporal ihdath: 'الإيجاد للشيء بعد أن لم يكن له وجود في زمان سابق' – bringing into existence after no existence in a prior time.
3
Extract the non-temporal meaning
Non-temporal ihdath: 'إفادة الشيء وجوداً، وذلك الشيء ليس له في ذاته ذلك الوجود، لا بحسب زمان دون زمان بل بحسب كل زمان' – giving existence to something that does not have it in itself, at all times.
The thing is constantly dependent on the giver.

Definition of Qidam: Three Types of Eternity
Author defines qidam in three ways: relative, absolute by time, and absolute by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between relative, absolute-by-time, and absolute-by-essence eternity, and identify which applies to the Creator.

Core Idea
Qidam distinguishes relative ancientness, infinite temporal past, and essential necessity without a cause.

The term qidam (قدم) is applied to things that are eternal or ancient. The author distinguishes three types: relative eternity (older than something else), absolute eternity in time (infinite past), and absolute eternity by essence (no beginning or cause, exclusive to God).

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term qidam in the Arabic. It is said to be used in several ways.
The passage begins with 'حد القدم'.
2
Extract relative qidam
Relative qidam: 'شيء زمانه في الماضي أكثر من زمان شيء آخر' – something whose past time is greater than another's.
This is comparative.
3
Extract absolute qidam by time
Absolute qidam by time: 'الشيء الذي وُجد في زمان ماض غير متناه' – something that existed in an infinite past time.
The heavens are given as an example according to philosophers.
4
Extract absolute qidam by essence
Absolute qidam by essence: 'الذي ليس لوجود ذاته مبدأ به وجب' – that which has no beginning for its existence by which it is necessitated, i.e., no cause.
This applies only to the Creator (al-Bari).

The Six Meanings of Sura (Form)
Author enumerates six distinct meanings of the term 'sura' with definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and distinguish the six meanings of the term 'sura' (form) as defined in Islamic logic and philosophy.

Core Idea
The term 'form' has six distinct meanings, from logical species to the separate human soul.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial homonym in Islamic logic and metaphysics: 'sura' (form). While the term appears frequently across texts, its meaning shifts depending on context. By memorizing and distinguishing these six definitions, you gain precision when reading philosophical works, especially in physics and logic. The author enumerates each meaning with its specific definiens, often sharing a core structure but with key variations.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core phrase for each meaning
For each of the six meanings, locate the Arabic definiens phrase that defines 'sura' in that sense. The author uses a consistent structure: 'حدّه بهذا المعنى...' followed by a description. Write down each definiens in Arabic and note the unique qualifiers.
2
Categorize the meanings by domain
Determine which meanings belong to logic (species), metaphysics (quiddity, perfection), physics (substantial form in matter), and psychology (soul). This categorization will help you recall the appropriate context for each meaning.
3
Compare the second and third meanings
Both are defined as 'every existent in a thing not as a part of it, and its constitution is not sound without it'. The difference is that the second specifies 'for its sake the thing exists' (teleological purpose), while the third does not. Note this subtle variation.
4
Identify the unique conditions in the fourth and fifth meanings
The fourth meaning includes 'cannot exist separate from the substrate' (inseparable from matter). The fifth meaning says 'the natural species is obtained by it', and the form itself cannot exist separate. Compare with the sixth meaning, which is explicitly separate.
5
Memorize the six meanings using a mnemonic
Create a mental list: 1) Logical species, 2) Teleological perfection, 3) Quiddity, 4) Inherent substantial form, 5) Constitutive species form, 6) Separate perfection (soul). Test yourself by recalling the definiens for each.
