The second art: On detailed definitions. And it may be said: nature for the element and the essential form. And the physicians apply the term nature to temperament, innate heat, structures of organs, motions, and the vegetative soul; each has another definition not relevant to the purpose, so we restricted ourselves to the first. Natural disposition (ṭabʿ): it is every disposition by which a species is perfected, whether active or passive, and it seems more general than nature. A thing may be by nature and not by natural disposition, like an extra finger; and it appears that it is by natural disposition according to individual nature, and not by natural disposition according to universal nature. And because natural disposition includes both active and passive, it is more general than nature, which is an active principle. Body (jism): a homonym; it may be applied to that which is named as being continuous, limited, extended in three dimensions potentially; I mean it is extended potentially even if not actually. And it may be applied to the form in which dimensions can occur in any relation as length, width, and depth, with determinate limits; this differs from the first in that if one does not require the whole to be limited and extended potentially or actually, or believes that the bodies of the world are infinite, then every part of them would be called body in this sense. And it is said body for a substance composed of hyle and form, and it is by the quality we mentioned, so it is called body in this sense. The difference between quantity and this form is that a piece of water or wax—whenever their shapes change, the limited extended dimensions change, and none of them remains numerically one, but the form receptive of these states remains numerically one without change. And the form receptive of these states is corporeality. Likewise, when a body condenses, like air turning into cloud or water by condensation, or rarefies, like ice when its corporeal form changes, and its dimensions and quantity change—this shows the difference between corporeal form, which belongs to the category of quantity, and form that belongs to the category of substance. Substance (jawhar): a homonym; it is said substance for an essence like man, or like whiteness, so one says 'the substance of whiteness' and its essence. And it is said substance for every existent whose essence does not need in existence another essence to accompany it to be actual; this is the meaning of their statement 'substance is self-subsistent'. And it is said for that which has this quality and can receive contraries successively, it is substance. And it is said substance for every essence whose existence is not in a subject, and this is the terminology of the ancient philosophers. And the difference between subject (mawḍūʿ) and locus (maḥall) has preceded, so the meaning of their statement 'existent not in a subject' is: an existent whose existence is not concomitant with a locus that is self-subsistent and constitutive of it. And it is permitted that...
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage opens a new section titled 'The Second Art: On Detailed Definitions'. It first revisits 'nature' (ṭabīʿa), stating it can be said for element and essential form, but notes that physicians use the term broadly for temperament, innate heat, etc.—the author restricts to the initial definition. Then it defines 'natural disposition' (ṭabʿ) as any disposition perfecting a species, whether active or passive; it is more general than nature. An extra finger is by nature (individual) but not by natural disposition (universal). Next, 'body' (jism) is a homonym with two main meanings: (1) a continuous limited extension in three dimensions potentially, (2) the form receptive of dimensions. The author distinguishes corporeal form from quantity: a piece of water/wax retains its corporeal form while dimensions change, showing corporeal form is not quantity. Finally, 'substance' (jawhar) is a homonym: it can mean essence (e.g., man, whiteness), self-subsistent existent, that which receives contraries, or that which exists not in a subject. The author references the prior distinction between subject and locus to explain the philosophers' definition. The passage ends mid-sentence.

الموضوع
المحل

Key Takeaway
The homonymy of jawhar requires careful context; the philosophers' technical definition is 'that which exists not in a subject'.

Distinguishing Nature and Disposition
Author defines nature and disposition, contrasting their scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between nature (طبيعة) and disposition (طبع) based on their definitions and scope as given in the passage.

Core Idea
Disposition is more general than nature because it includes both action and passion.

In this passage, the author defines two closely related terms: nature and disposition. Understanding their precise meanings is crucial for grasping later discussions in natural philosophy and logic. The key distinction lies in the scope: disposition encompasses both active and passive qualities, while nature is limited to a principle of action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms defined
Find the two terms in the Arabic: طبيعة (nature) and طبع (disposition). Note that the author also mentions medical usage of nature but sets it aside.
2
Extract the core definition of nature
The author defines nature philosophically as the element and essential form. He then notes physicians use it differently, but the focus is on the first meaning.
The definition is: 'the element and essential form'.
3
Extract the core definition of disposition
Disposition is defined as every quality by which a species is perfected, whether active or passive.
This includes both فعلية (active) and انفعالية (passive).
4
Compare the scope
The author explicitly states that disposition is more general (أعم) than nature because it covers both action and passion, while nature is only a principle of action.
Nature is مبدأ فِعلي (principle of action).
5
Analyze the example
An extra finger occurs by nature (as a natural growth) but not by disposition (since it does not perfect the species). This illustrates the difference.
The example shows that something can be natural without being dispositional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثاني : في الحدود المفصلة

The second art: On detailed definitions. And it may be said: nature (tabi'a) is said of the element and the essential form. Commentary
This passage defines 'nature' (طبيعة) and 'disposition' (طبع). The author first distinguishes between the philosophical usage of nature (as element and essential form) and the broader medical usage. He then defines disposition as a quality that perfects a species, covering both active and passive states. The key point is that disposition is more general than nature because it encompasses both action and passion, whereas nature is specifically a principle of action. The example of an extra finger illustrates something caused by nature (as a principle) but not by disposition (since it does not perfect the species).

Distinguishing the Three Senses of Jism (Body)
Author defines body as a homonymous term with multiple meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three distinct meanings of 'jism' (body) as presented in the passage and explain the difference between quantity and corporeal form.

Core Idea
Body is a homonym; its meanings range from potential extension to a substance composed of matter and form.

In this passage, the author unpacks the term 'jism' (body), showing that it is not a single concept but a homonym (mushtarak) used in at least three distinct ways. Understanding these distinctions is critical for precise reasoning in kalam and falsafa, where discussions of physical bodies, atoms, and substance rely on the correct sense of 'body.' We'll break down each sense and test the distinction with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the homonym
The passage begins by stating that 'jism' is a homonymous term. Note that homonyms have multiple meanings; the task is to extract them.
2
Extract the three meanings
From the passage, list the three senses: (a) a continuous bounded extension in three dimensions potentially; (b) a form in which dimensions can occur; (c) a substance composed of matter and form.
The first sense applies even if only potentially extended; the second is about the form receptive of dimensions.
3
See the difference between (a) and (b)
Meaning (a) refers to the extended thing itself (e.g., a body of water). Meaning (b) refers to the form that can receive dimensions, which can exist even if the body is not actually bounded (e.g., if bodies were infinite, every part would count as a body in sense (a), but sense (b) does not require actual extension).
4
Understand the third meaning
Meaning (c) defines body as a substance (جوهر) composed of matter and form. This is the philosophical sense that underlies the other two.
5
Apply the key test: quantity vs. corporeal form
The passage contrasts quantity (kamm) with corporeal form (sura jismiyya). When wax changes shape, its dimensions (quantity) change, but the corporeal form remains one in number. So corporeal form is not the dimensions themselves but the receptive principle.
This distinction is crucial to avoid confusing the category of quantity with the form that belongs to substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجسم : اسم مشترك قد يُطلق على المُسمَّى به من حيث إنه متصل محدود ممسوح في أبعاد ثلاثة بالقوة؛ أعني أنه ممسوح بالقوة وإن لم يكن بالفعل. وقد لصورة يمكن أن يعرض فيها أبعاد كيف نسبت طولاً وعرضاً وعمقاً، ذات حدود متعيّنة، وهذا يفارق الأول في أنه لو لم يشترط كون الجملة محدوداً ممسوحاً بالقوة أو بالفعل أو اعتقد أن أجسام العالم لا نهاية لها، لكان كلّ جزء منها يُسمّى جسماً بهذا الاعتبار، ويقال : جسم لجوهر مؤلف من هيولى وصورة، وهو بالصفة التي ذكرناها فَتُسمّى جسماً بهذا الاعتبار والفرق بين الكم وهذه الصورة أن قطعة الماء والشمع من كلّما بدّلت أشكالها تبدلت فيها الأبعاد المحدودة الممسوحة، ولم يبق واحد منها بعينه واحداً بالعدد وبقيت الصورة القابلة لهذه الأحوال واحدة بالعدد من غير تبدّل. والصورة القابلة لهذه الأحوال هي جسمية، وكذلك إذا تكاثف الجسم مثلاً كانقلاب الهواء بالتكاثف سحاباً أو ماء، أو تخلخل مثلاً الجمد لما يستحيل صورته الجسمية، واستحال أبعاده ومقداره، ولهذا يظهر الفرق بين الصورة الجسمية التي هي من باب الكم وبين الصورة التي هي من باب الجوهر .
Body (jism): is a homonymous term. It may be applied to what is named by it insofar as it is continuous, bounded, extended in three dimensions potentially; i.e., extended potentially even if not actually. And it may be applied to a form in which dimensions can occur, however related as length, width, depth, with definite limits. This differs from the first in that if it is not stipulated that the whole is bounded and extended potentially or actually, or if one believes that the bodies of the world are infinite, then every part of it would be called body in this sense. And it is said: body is a substance composed of matter and form, and it is with the description we mentioned, so it is called body in this sense. The difference between quantity and this form is that a piece of water or wax whenever their shapes change, the limited extended dimensions change in them, and not one of them remains the same in number, while the form receptive of these states remains one in number without change. And the form receptive of these states is corporeality. Similarly, when a body condenses, like the transformation of air by condensation into cloud or water, or rarefies like ice when its corporeal form changes and its dimensions and magnitude change, and hence the difference appears between the corporeal form which belongs to quantity and the form which belongs to substance.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage defines 'body' (جسم) as a homonym with multiple meanings: (1) a continuous bounded extension in three dimensions potentially; (2) a form that can receive dimensions; and (3) a substance composed of matter and form. The author distinguishes corporeality (الصورة الجسمية) from quantity by noting that quantity changes with shape, whereas the corporeal form remains constant. Examples of water, wax, and air condensation illustrate this distinction. The corporeal form is of the category of quantity, while the substantial form is of substance.

كم

The Four Senses of Substance (Jawhar)
Author defines substance as a homonym with four senses, then applies them to various entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four senses of jawhar and apply them to determine which entities qualify as substance in which sense.

Core Idea
Substance is not a single concept but a homonym with four senses: essence, self-subsistence, receptivity, and not-in-a-subject.

The term 'jawhar' (substance) is central to Islamic metaphysics, but its meaning is not monolithic. This passage clarifies that it is a homonym with four distinct senses. Understanding these senses is essential for reading philosophical and theological texts that discuss the nature of beings, from accidents to the First Principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the homonym
The author begins by stating that 'jawhar' is a mushtarak (homonym). Notice that he lists four distinct definitions introduced by 'yuqāl' (it is said). This sets the stage for a polysemous term.
2
Extract each sense
For each 'yuqāl', write down the defining characteristic: (1) essence (dhāt), e.g., jawhar of whiteness; (2) self-subsistent being (qā'im bi-nafsih); (3) receiver of opposites; (4) not in a subject (lā fī mawḍū‘). These are the four senses.
3
Distinguish subject from locus
The passage refers to a previous distinction between subject (mawḍū‘) and locus (maḥall). An accident can be in a locus without being in a subject. The fourth sense (not in a subject) allows for being in a locus if that locus is not constitutive. Grasping this distinction is key to applying the fourth sense.
The author mentions 'the difference between subject and locus has preceded' — this is a reference to earlier material.
4
Apply the senses to various entities
The author tests each entity: whiteness etc. are substance only in the first sense (essence). The First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) is substance in all senses except the third (since He does not receive opposites). Hyle (matter) is substance in the fourth and third senses. Form is substance in the fourth sense only. Follow these applications carefully.
5
Contrast with theological usage
The theologians restrict 'substance' to the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), excluding even the First Principle. The author dismisses such quibbling once meanings are clear. Note how the theological definition differs from the philosophical one.
This highlights the importance of disambiguating terms in scholarly discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجوهر اسم مشترك يقال : جوهر لذات كالإنسان، أو كالبياض فيقال : جوهر البياض وذاته ويقال : جوهر لكلّ موجود وذاته لا يحتاج في الوجود إلى ذات أخرى تقارنها حتى يكون بالفعل، وهو معنى قولهم : الجوهر قائم بنفسه، ويقال : لما كان بهذه الصفة وكان من شأنه أن يقبل الأضداد بتعاقبها عليه، جوهر. ويقال : جوهر لكلّ ذات وجوده ليس في موضوع، وعليه اصطلاح الفلاسفة القدماء. وقد سبق الفرق بين الموضوع والمحلّ فيكون معنى قولهم: الموجود لا في موضوع. الموجود غير مقارن الوجود لمحل قائم بنفسه مقوم له، ولا بأس بأن يكون في محل لا يتقوم المحلّ دونه بالفعل فإنه وإن كان في محل فليس في موضوع، فكلّ موجود إن كان كالبياض والحرارة والحركة والعلم فهو جوهر بالمعنى الأول، والمبدأ الأول جوهر بالمعاني كلها إلا بالوجه الثالث وهو تعاقب الأضداد . نعم قد يتحاشى عن إطلاق لفظ الجوهر عليه تأدباً من حيث الشرع، والهيولى جوهر بالمعنى الرابع والثالث، وليس جوهراً بالمعنى الثاني، والصورة جوهر بالمعنى الرابع وليس جوهراً بالمعنى الثاني والثالث، والمتكلمون يخصصون اسم الجوهر بالجوهر الفرد المتحيّز الذي لا ينقسم، ويسمون المنقسم جسماً لا جوهراً، وبحكم ذلك يمتنعون عن إطلاق اسم الجوهر على المبدأ الأول عزّ وجلّ، والمشاحة في الأسماء بعد إيضاح المعاني دأب ذوي القصور.
Substance (jawhar) is a homonymous term. It is said: jawhar for an essence like human, or like whiteness, so it is said: the substance of whiteness and its essence. And it is said: jawhar for every existent whose essence does not need in existence another essence to accompany it in order to be actual—this is the meaning of their statement: substance is self-subsistent. And it is said: for that which has this description and is such that it receives opposites successively, it is jawhar. And it is said: jawhar for every essence whose existence is not in a subject; this is the terminology of the ancient philosophers. The difference between subject and locus has preceded, so the meaning of their statement: 'the existent not in a subject' is: the existent not accompanying in existence a locus that is self-subsistent and constitutive of it. It is acceptable that it be in a locus that is not constituted by it in actuality, for even if it is in a locus, it is not in a subject. So every existent, if it is like whiteness, heat, movement, or knowledge, it is substance in the first sense. The First Principle is substance in all senses except the third, which is succession of opposites. However, one may avoid applying the term 'substance' to Him out of propriety from the perspective of the Law. Hyle is substance in the fourth and third senses, not in the second. Form is substance in the fourth sense, not in the second or third. The theologians specify the term 'substance' to the indivisible atom that occupies space, and they call the divisible 'body' not 'substance'. By that ruling they refrain from applying the term 'substance' to the First Principle, Blessed and Exalted. Quibbling over names after clarifying meanings is the habit of those with deficiency.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage defines 'substance' (جوهر) in four senses: (1) essence of a thing (like human or whiteness), (2) self-subsistent existent (قائم بنفسه), (3) that which receives opposites, (4) not in a subject. The author explains each and then applies them to different entities: whiteness, heat, etc., are substance in the first sense; the First Principle is substance in all but the third (since He does not receive opposites); hyle is substance in the fourth and third; form in the fourth only. The theologians' usage is different, limiting substance to the indivisible atom. The author concludes that such verbal disputes are pointless once meanings are clear.

موضوع
محل
هيولى
صورة

Key Takeaway

The Six Senses of Accident
Author defines accident as a homonym with six senses, applying to different entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six senses of the term 'arad (accident) and identify which sense applies to a given example from the passage.

Core Idea
Accident is a homonym with six distinct logical and metaphysical senses.

In logic, one term can carry multiple meanings. The author here unpacks the term 'arad (accident) into six precise senses, each corresponding to a different way something can be accidental. Understanding these senses is crucial for correctly interpreting theological and scientific discussions about substances and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the homonym
Notice that the author begins by stating 'arad is a homonym (ism mushtarak). The entire passage is devoted to distinguishing its meanings.
2
List the six senses
Each sense is introduced with the phrase 'ويقال: عرض لـ...' (and it is said: 'arad for...). Identify all six definitions given in the text.
The senses are: (1) existent in a locus, (2) existent in a subject, (3) universal non-constitutive predicate, (4) meaning outside nature, (5) predicated due to another, (6) non-original existence.
3
Compare first two senses
Distinguish between 'existent in a locus' (maḥall) and 'existent in a subject' (mawḍū‘). What is the difference? The locus is less constitutive than the subject.
4
Understand the third sense
The third sense is the universal non-constitutive predicate, which is contrasted with the essential (dhātī) in the book *Preludes to the Syllogism*.
5
Note the remaining senses
The fourth, fifth, and sixth senses deal with existence outside nature, predication due to another, and non-original existence. These are more metaphysical in nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العَرَضُ : اسم مشترك فيقال لكلّ موجود في محل عرض، ويقال: عرض موجود في موضوع، ويقال : عرض للمعنى الكلّي المفرد المحمول على كثيرين حملا غير مقوم، وهو العَرَض الذي قابلناه بالذاتي في كتاب مقدمات القياس. ويقال : عَرَض لكلّ معنى موجود للشيء خارج عن طبعه، ويقال : عَرَض لكلّ معنى يحمل على الشيء لأجل وجوده في آخر يفارقه، ويقال: عرض لكلّ معنى وجوده في أول الأمر لا يكون ؛ فالصورة عرض بالمعنى الأول فقط، وهو الذي يعنيه المتكلم إذا ما قابله بالجوهر والأبيض، أي الشيء ذو البياض الذي يحمل على الثلج والجص والكافور ليس هو عَرَضاً بالوجه الأول والثاني، وهو عَرَضٌ بالوجه الثالث، وذلك لأن هذا الأبيض الذي هو نوع محمول غير مقوم، وهو جوهر ليس في موضوع ولا محلّ، فالبياض هو الحال في محل وموضوع، والبياض لا يحمل على الثلج فلا ثلج بياض، بل يقال : أبيض، ومعناه أنه شيء ذو أبيض فلا يكون هذا حملاً مقوماً. وحركة الحجر إلى أسفل عرض بالوجه الأول والثاني والثالث، وليس عَرَضاً بالوجه الرابع والخامس والسادس، بل حركته إلى هذه الوجوه، وحركة القاعد في السفينة عَرَض بالوجه السادس فوق عَرَض بجميع والرابع .
Accident ('arad) is a homonymous term. It is said: 'arad for every existent in a locus. And it is said: 'arad for an existent in a subject. And it is said: 'arad for a universal single meaning predicated of many without constitutive predication—this is the accident we contrasted with the essential in the book *Preludes to the Syllogism*. And it is said: 'arad for every meaning existing for a thing outside its nature. And it is said: 'arad for every meaning predicated of a thing due to its existence in another distinct from it. And it is said: 'arad for every meaning whose existence in the first instance is not. So form is an accident only in the first sense, which is what the theologian means when he contrasts it with substance. And white (the thing having whiteness) which is predicated of snow, plaster, and camphor is not an accident in the first and second senses, but is an accident in the third sense. This is because this white is a non-constitutive universal, and it is a substance not in a subject or locus. For whiteness is what inheres in a locus and subject, and whiteness is not predicated of snow, so snow is not whiteness; rather it is said 'white', meaning it is a thing having whiteness, so this is not constitutive predication. The motion of a stone downwards is an accident in the first, second, and third senses, not in the fourth, fifth, and sixth; rather its motion is to these faces. And the motion of a stationary person in a ship is an accident in the sixth sense, above accident in all and the fourth.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines 'accident' (عرض) in six distinct senses: (1) existent in a locus, (2) existent in a subject, (3) universal non-constitutive predicate, (4) meaning existing outside the nature of a thing, (5) meaning predicated due to existence in another, (6) meaning whose existence is not original. The author applies these to examples: form is accident only in the first sense; 'white' as a thing (e.g., snow) is accident in the third sense only; motion of a stone is accident in senses 1–3; motion of a stationary person in a ship is accident in the sixth sense (and perhaps fourth). The complex discussion illustrates how a single term can refer to different logical and metaphysical categories.

موضوع

D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The term 'arad has six distinct technical senses, and each sense applies to different kinds of entities.

Applying the Senses: Form, White, and Motion
Author defines accident as a homonym with six senses, applying to different entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify given entities (form, white, motion) under the appropriate sense(s) of accident according to the passage.

Core Idea
The same entity may be an accident in some senses but not others.

The author illustrates the six senses with concrete examples: form, white (the thing having whiteness), whiteness itself, motion of a stone, and motion of a stationary person. By analyzing these examples, we learn how the senses are applied and how to avoid conflating different meanings.

Guided Path
1
Examine the example of form
The passage says form (ṣūra) is accident only in the first sense. Why? Because form is an existent in a locus (the matter) but not a subject. Theologians contrast form with substance.
2
Examine the example of 'white'
'White' (abyaḍ) as a thing (e.g., snow, plaster) is an accident only in the third sense. It is not an accident in the first two senses because the thing itself is a substance.
3
Differentiate 'white' and 'whiteness'
The passage explicitly distinguishes: 'white' is a non-constitutive universal predicate, while whiteness (bayāḍ) is what inheres in a locus and subject. Therefore, whiteness is an accident in the first and second senses.
4
Analyze the motion of a stone
The motion of a stone downwards is an accident in the first three senses. It is not an accident in the fourth, fifth, or sixth senses. So it fits all three definitions: it exists in a locus, in a subject, and is a non-constitutive predicate.
5
Analyze the motion of a stationary person
The passage says this motion is an accident in the sixth sense (non-original existence) and perhaps also the fourth. The text is slightly garbled, but it shows that not all motions are accidents in the same senses.

Distinguishing 'White' from 'Whiteness'
Author distinguishes between 'white' (thing) and 'whiteness' (property) as different types of accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'white' (the thing) is not an accident in the same sense as 'whiteness' (the property) according to the passage.

Core Idea
'White' is a substance that has whiteness, not whiteness itself.

A common confusion in logic is conflating the predicate 'white' with the property 'whiteness'. The author carefully distinguishes them: 'white' (the thing) is a non-constitutive universal predicate (third sense), but it is not an inhering accident because the thing itself is a substance. 'Whiteness' is the accident that inheres in a subject. This distinction is critical for accurate predication and understanding theological discussions about attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms: 'white' and 'whiteness'
The passage uses 'abyaḍ' (white) to refer to the thing having whiteness, and 'bayāḍ' (whiteness) for the property. Note the difference in Arabic endings.
2
Determine the sense of 'white' as accident
The passage states that 'white' is an accident only in the third sense (universal non-constitutive predicate). It is not accident in the first or second senses because it is a substance, not something inhering in a subject.
3
Determine the sense of 'whiteness' as accident
The passage says 'whiteness is what inheres in a locus and subject' (البياض هو الحال في محل وموضوع). Therefore whiteness is an accident in the first and second senses.
4
Understand why 'white' is not in the first two senses
Because 'white' refers to a substance (e.g., snow) that has whiteness. That substance is not a property inhering in something else; it is a subject. So it cannot be an accident in the sense of 'existent in a subject'.
5
Appreciate the implications for predication
When we say 'Snow is white', the predicate 'white' is a non-constitutive universal. It does not mean snow is the accident whiteness. This distinction avoids category errors.

Source Text

Celestial Sphere and Planet: Definitions and Key Properties
Author defines the celestial sphere and planet as simple spherical bodies with distinct properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the genus and differentia of both the celestial sphere (falak) and planet (kawkab) as defined in Islamic philosophy, and compare their shared and distinguishing attributes.

Core Idea
Two celestial bodies, one essence: both are simple spheres, but only one encompasses the center.

In this passage, the author gives concise definitions of two fundamental celestial entities: the falak and the kawkab. These definitions are not just memorization items—they encode the entire Ptolemaic cosmology in a few lines. Understanding the shared attributes (simple body, spherical, eternal) and the key difference (encompassing vs. not encompassing the center) will help you grasp how Islamic philosophers conceptualized the heavens. Let's break down these definitions into their logical components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared attributes
Read both definitions. List the properties common to both the sphere and the planet: simple body (jism basīṭ), spherical (kurrī), eternal (ghayr qābil lil-kawn wa-l-fasād), naturally moving around the center (mutaharrik bi-l-ṭabʿ ʿalā l-wasaṭ).
2
Extract the genus
Both are classified as 'simple bodies' (jism basīṭ). This is the genus—a body not composed of the four elements, distinct from sublunar matter. Recognise this as the overarching category.
3
Find the differentia of the sphere
The sphere is distinguished by 'encompassing the center' (mushtamil ʿalayhi). Its volume surrounds the center. This is the specific difference that sets it apart from the planet.
4
Find the differentia of the planet
The planet is distinguished by 'not encompassing the center' (ghayr mushtamil ʿalayhi) and that its natural place is the sphere itself. It moves around the center but does not envelop it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلك : عندهم جسم بسيط كري غير قابل للكون والفساد، متحرّك بالطبع على الوسط مشتمل عليه .
الكوكب جسم بسيط كري مكانه الطبيعي نفس الفلك، من شأنه أن يكون غیر قابل للكون والفساد متحرّك على الوسط غير مشتمل عليه .
The celestial sphere (falak): according to them, it is a simple spherical body not subject to generation and corruption, moving by nature around the center, encompassing it. The planet (kawkab): a simple spherical body whose natural place is the sphere itself, not subject to generation and corruption, moving around the center but not encompassing it.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage gives concise definitions of the celestial sphere and the planet. Both are simple (uncomposed) spherical bodies, eternal (not subject to generation/corruption), and move naturally around the center of the universe. The difference is that the sphere encompasses the center (i.e., its volume surrounds it), while the planet does not encompass the center—it only moves around it. This reflects the Ptolemaic model adopted by Islamic philosophers.

Key Terms
فلك
كوكب
جسم بسيط
كون وفساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true of both the sphere and the planet according to the passage?
A) They encompass the center.
B) They are composite bodies.
C) They are not subject to generation and corruption.
D) They are slow-moving.

Key Takeaway
The celestial sphere and planet share the same genus (simple body) but differ in whether they encompass the center; this distinction is foundational for the Ptolemaic cosmos.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Definitions of Celestial Bodies: Falak, Kawkab, Shams, Qamar
Al-Ghazali defines the celestial sphere, planet, sun, and moon with their essential properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus, differentia, and definition type for each celestial body as defined by al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali defines celestial bodies using genus (simple spherical body) and differentiae (location, motion, properties).

In this passage, al-Ghazali provides definitions of four key celestial bodies from Aristotelian cosmology. These definitions follow the pattern of hadd (complete definition) consisting of genus and differentia. By analyzing them, you will learn how definitions in the Islamic tradition capture essential properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common genus
Notice that all four definitions begin with 'simple spherical body' (jism basit kuri). This is the genus that unifies celestial bodies.
The genus is the same for all, but each has a different differentia.
2
Extract the differentia for each
For falak: eternal, naturally moves around and encompasses the center. For kawkab: natural place is the sphere, does not encompass the center. For sun: largest, brightest, fourth sphere. For moon: lowest sphere, receives light, dark color.
Differentiae distinguish each species within the genus.
3
Classify each definition
Determine whether each definition is a hadd haqiqi (real definition) or rasm (description). All appear to be hadd haqiqi as they state essential properties.
A hadd haqiqi includes genus and essential differentia.
4
Compare definitions for consistency
Check that the definitions are mutually exclusive: falak versus kawkab are distinguished by encompassing; sun and moon are both kawkab but differ by location and properties.
Consistency ensures the definitions cover all intended instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلك : عندهم جسم بسيط كري غير قابل للكون والفساد، متحرّك بالطبع على الوسط مشتمل عليه . الكوكب جسم بسيط كري مكانه الطبيعي نفس الفلك، من شأنه أن يكون غير قابل للكون والفساد متحرّك على الوسط غير مشتمل عليه . الشمس : كوكب هو أعظم الكواكب كلها جرماً وأشدها ضوءاً، ومكانه الطبيعي في الكرة الرابعة . القمر : هو كوكب مكانه الطبيعي في الأسفل من شأنه أن يقبل النور من الشمس على أشكال مختلفة، ولونه الذاتي إلى السواد.
The celestial sphere (falak), in their view, is a simple spherical body, not subject to generation and corruption, naturally moving around the center, encompassing it. The planet (kawkab) is a simple spherical body whose natural place is the celestial sphere itself; its attribute is to be not subject to generation and corruption, moving around the center not encompassing it. The sun is a planet that is the greatest of all planets in mass and the most intense in light; its natural place is in the fourth sphere. The moon is a planet whose natural place is below; its attribute is to receive light from the sun in various figures, and its intrinsic color tends toward black.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage provides definitions of four celestial bodies from Aristotelian cosmology. Al-Ghazali first defines al-falak (the celestial sphere) as a simple (uncompounded) spherical body that is not subject to generation and corruption (i.e., it is eternal), naturally moves around the center (the earth), and encompasses it. Then al-kawkab (planet) is similar but differs in that its natural place is the sphere itself (it resides within it), and it does not encompass the center; it is also eternal and moves circularly. The sun (al-shams) is a species of kawkab distinguished by being the largest and brightest, located in the fourth celestial sphere. The moon (al-qamar) is also a kawkab, located in the lowest sphere (closest to earth), receives light from the sun in varying phases, and has a naturally dark color. The common genus for all is 'simple spherical body,' and the differentiae are based on location, motion, and properties.

Key Terms
فلك
كوكب
جسم بسيط
كري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, what distinguishes the falak from a kawkab?
A) Falak is eternal, kawkab is not.
B) Falak encompasses the center, kawkab does not.
C) Falak is larger than kawkab.
D) Falak is not spherical.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali uses the genus 'simple spherical body' and differentiae of location and motion to define celestial bodies, exemplifying the hadd method.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Provide a definition for 'earth' (al-ard) using the same pattern: genus simple spherical body, differentia: stationary at the center, not moving around it.
?
2. Identify which part of the definition of the sun is genus and which is differentia, and explain why the differentia distinguishes it from other planets.

Hadd and Rasm
beginner
5 min
The Four Elements: Fire, Air, Water, Earth
Al-Ghazali defines the four elements with their qualities, natural motion, and places.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four elements by their essential qualities and natural motions.

Core Idea
Each element is a simple body defined by a unique pair of primary qualities and a natural place.

Al-Ghazali opens his discussion of the physical world by defining the four simple bodies that compose everything beneath the moon. Each element is defined by a pair of primary qualities (hot/cold, moist/dry), its natural motion, and its location. These definitions are not arbitrary; they are derived from observable physical behavior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
Notice that each element is introduced as 'jism basit' (simple body). This is the common genus.
2
Extract the differentia
For each element, find the pair of primary qualities (e.g., fire: hot and dry). Also note the natural motion (e.g., fire moves away from center).
3
Determine the natural place
Each element has a designated location (fire under lunar sphere, air between fire and earth, etc.). This is part of the definition.
4
Compare the elements
Contrast fire and air (both hot, but fire dry, air moist) and water and earth (both cold, but water moist, earth dry).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النار : جسم بسيط طباعه أن يكون حاراً يابساً متحركاً بالطبع عن الوسط، يستقرّ تحت كرة القمر . الهواء: جرم بسيط طباعه أن يكون حاراً رطباً مُشِفاً لطيفاً متحركاً إلى المكان الذي تحت كرة النار فوق كرة الأرض . الماء : جرم بسيط طباعه أن يكون بارداً رطباً مُشفّاً متحركاً إلى المكان الذي تحت كرة الهواء وفوق الأرض . الأرض: جسم بسيط طباعه أن يكون بارداً يابساً متحركاً إلى الوسط نازلاً فيه . العالم : هو مجموع الأجسام الطبيعية البسيطة كلها. ويقال : عالم لكل جملة موجودات متجانسة، كقولهم: عالم الطبيعة وعالم النفس، وعالم العقل. الحركة : كمال أول بالقوة من جهة ما هو بالقوة وإن شئت قلت: هو خروج من القوة إلى الفعل لا في آن واحد وكلّ تغيّر عندهم يُسمّى حركة. وأما حركة الكلّ فهو حركة الجرم الأقصى على الوسط مشتملة على جميع الحركات التي على الوسط وأسرع منها. الدهر : هو المعنى المعقول من إضافة الثبات إلى النفس في الزمان كله. الزمان : هو مقدار الحركة موسوم من جهة التقدم والتأخر . الآن : هو ظرف يشترك فيه الماضي والمستقبل من الزمان، وقد يقال: إن الزمان صغير المقدار عن الوهم متصل بالآن الحقيقي من جنسه . المكان : هو السطح الباطن من الجوهر الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من الجسم المحوي. وقد يقال : مكان للسطح الأسفل الذي يستقر عليه شيء يقله، ويقال : مكان بمعنى ثالث إلا أنه غير موجود، وهو أبعاد متناهية كأبعاد المتمكن يدخل فيها أبعاد المتمكّن ، وإن كان يجوز أن يُلْقَى من غير متمكن كان وإن كان لا يجوز إلا أن يشغلها جسم موجود فيه فليس بخلاء. الخلاء : بُعْد يمكن أن يفرض فيه أبعاد ثلاثة قوائم لا في مادة، من شأنه أن
Fire: a simple body whose nature is to be hot and dry, naturally moving away from the center, and it settles under the sphere of the moon. Air: a simple body whose nature is to be hot and moist, transparent, subtle, moving to the place that is under the sphere of fire and above the sphere of earth. Water: a simple body whose nature is to be cold and moist, transparent, moving to the place that is under the sphere of air and above the earth. Earth: a simple body whose nature is to be cold and dry, moving toward the center, descending into it. The universe (al-‘alam): is the totality of all simple natural bodies. And it is also said of every collection of homogeneous existents, as they say: the world of nature, the world of the soul, and the world of intellect. Motion: is the first entelechy of a potential thing qua potential; or if you wish, it is the emergence from potentiality to actuality not in a single instant. And every change, in their view, is called motion. As for the motion of the whole, it is the motion of the outermost body around the center, encompassing all motions that are around the center and faster than them. Eternity (al-dahr): is the intelligible meaning from the addition of permanence to the soul over all time. Time (al-zaman): is the measure of motion, marked by before and after. The instant (al-an): is a temporal container that the past and future of time share; and it is said that time is a small amount in estimation, connected to the real instant of its kind. Place (al-makan): is the inner surface of the containing substance that touches the outer surface of the contained body. And it is also said of the lower surface upon which something rests that carries it. And it is said in a third sense (which does not exist): finite dimensions like the dimensions of the occupant, which the dimensions of the occupant enter into; and if it were permissible to be thrown without an occupant, it would be void; but it is not permissible except that a body exists in it occupies it; so it is not void. Void (al-khala’): a dimension in which three perpendicular dimensions can be assumed without matter; its attribute is that...
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a series of definitions of key concepts in Aristotelian physics. Al-Ghazali begins with the four elements: fire (hot-dry, moves away from center, located under lunar sphere), air (hot-moist, transparent, subtle, located between fire and earth), water (cold-moist, transparent, located between air and earth), earth (cold-dry, moves to center). Each is a 'simple body' with a natural place and motion. He then defines the universe (al-‘alam) as the totality of all simple natural bodies, but also notes a broader usage: any collection of homogeneous existents (e.g., world of nature, soul, intellect). Motion (al-haraka) is defined in two ways: as first entelechy of the potential qua potential (Aristotelian definition), or as emergence from potentiality to actuality not in an instant. Every change is called motion. The motion of the whole (the outermost sphere) encompasses all other circular motions and is fastest. Eternity (al-dahr) is a concept of permanence in the soul over all time. Time (al-zaman) is the measure of motion with respect to before and after. The instant (al-an) is a point shared by past and future. Place (al-makan) is defined as the inner boundary of the containing body, but also as the surface on which something rests, and a third hypothetical sense (void). Void (al-khala’) is defined as a dimension that can be imagined without matter, but the definition is cut off.

A) Hot and dry

C) Cold and dry
D) Cold and moist

Key Takeaway
Each element is a simple body defined by a pair of primary qualities and a natural motion to its proper place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Write out a definition for earth (al-ard) in the form: 'Earth is a [genus] whose nature is to be [quality1] and [quality2], moving [direction].' Then check if it matches Al-Ghazali's definition.
?
2. Compare the definition of water with that of air. Which qualities are shared? Which differ? How does the natural place differ?
